{"code":1,"isDone":false,"toInsertArticleList":[{"createdTime":"2020-08-24 22:49:54","updatedTime":"2020-08-24 22:49:54","title":"Robotic Chameleon Tongue Snatches Nearby Objects in the Blink of an Eye","link":"https://spectrum.ieee.org/tech-talk/robotics/robotics-hardware/robotic-chameleon-tongue-snatches-objects","description":"<link href=\"/ns/interactive/0118race-to-5g/css/5g-logo-treatments.css\" rel=\"stylesheet\"> \n <div class=\"mobileHide\"> \n  <div class=\"imgWrapper offsetLeft lt sm\"> \n   <a href=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/static/journal-watch\"><img alt=\"Journal Watch report logo, link to report landing page\" src=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzI0MTAwOQ.jpeg\" /></a> \n  </div> \n </div> \n <p>Chameleons may be slow-moving lizards, but their tongues can <a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=pn37lT7HbrA\">accelerate at astounding speeds</a>,&nbsp;snatching&nbsp;insects before they have any chance of fleeing. Inspired by this remarkable skill, researchers<span>&nbsp;in South Korea&nbsp;</span>have developed a robotic tongue that springs forth quickly to snatch up nearby items.</p> \n <p>They envision the tool, called Snatcher, being used by drones and robots that need to collect items without getting too close to them. “For example, a quadrotor with this manipulator will be able to snatch distant targets, instead of hovering and picking up,” explains&nbsp;Gwang-Pil Jung, a researcher at <span>Seoul National University of Science and Technology&nbsp;(SeoulTech)</span>&nbsp;who co-designed the new device.</p> \n <p><iframe allowfullscreen=\"\" frameborder=\"0\" height=\"349\" src=\"//www.youtube.com/embed/EefoItNaNms\" width=\"620\"></iframe></p> \n <p>There has been&nbsp;<a href=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/automaton/robotics/industrial-robots/japanese-researchers-developing-robotic-chameleon-tongue-first\">other research into robotic chameleon tongues</a>, but what’s unique about&nbsp;Snatcher&nbsp;is that it packs chameleon-tongue fast snatching performance into a form factor that’s portable—the total size is 12&nbsp;x 8.5 x 8.5 centimeters and it weighs under 120 grams. Still, it’s able to fast snatch up to 30 grams from 80 <span>centimeters</span> away in under 600 milliseconds.&nbsp;</p> \n <figure class=\"rt med-lrg\" role=\"img\"> \n  <img alt=\"The robotic chameleon tongue mechanism\" src=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3NDYxOQ.jpeg\"> \n   <figcaption class=\"hi-cap\">\n     Image: SeoulTech \n   </figcaption> \n   <figcaption>\n     The fast snatching deployable arm is powered by a wind-up spring attached to a motor (a series elastic actuator) combined with an active clutch. The clutch is what allows the single spring to drive both the shooting and the retracting.&nbsp; \n   </figcaption> </img> \n </figure> \n <p>To create Snatcher, Jung and a colleague at SeoulTech, Dong-Jun Lee, set about developing a spring-like device that’s controlled by an active clutch combined with a single series elastic actuator. Powered by a&nbsp;wind-up spring, a steel tapeline—analogous to a chameleon’s tongue—passes through two geared feeders. <span>The clutch is what allows the single spring unwinding in one direction to drive both the shooting and the retracting,&nbsp;by switching a geared wheel between driving the forward feeder or the backward feeder.</span></p> \n <p>The end result is a lightweight snatching device that can retrieve an object 0.8 meters away within 600 milliseconds. Jung notes that some other, existing devices designed for retrieval are capable of accomplishing the task quicker, at about 300 milliseconds, but these designs tend to be bulky.&nbsp;A more detailed description of Snatcher was <a href=\"https://ieeexplore.ieee.org/document/9145650\">published</a> July 21 in <em><a href=\"https://ieeexplore.ieee.org/xpl/RecentIssue.jsp?punumber=7083369\">IEEE Robotics and Automation Letters</a></em>.</p> \n <figure class=\"xlrg\" role=\"img\"> \n  <img alt=\"Drone\" src=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3NDYyMA.jpeg\"> \n   <figcaption class=\"hi-cap\">\n     Photo: Dong-Jun Lee and Gwang-Pil Jung/SeoulTech \n   </figcaption> \n   <figcaption>\n     Snatcher’s&nbsp;relative small size&nbsp;means that it can be installed on a DJI Phantom drone. The researchers want to find out if their system can help make&nbsp;package delivery or retrieval faster and safer. \n   </figcaption> </img> \n </figure> \n <p>“Our final goal is to install the Snatcher to a commercial drone and achieve meaningful work, such as grasping packages,” says Jung. One&nbsp;of the challenges they still need to address is how to&nbsp;power the actuation system more efficiently. “To solve this issue, we are finding materials having high energy density.”&nbsp;Another improvement is designing a chameleon tongue-like gripper, replacing the simple hook that’s currently used to pick&nbsp;up objects. “We are planning to make a bi-stable gripper to passively grasp a target object as soon as the gripper contacts the object,” says Jung.</p> \n <div class=\"mobileShow\"> \n  <h3 class=\"RptHdBackBarMobile\"><span class=\"BackArrowBlkBkgrd\">&lt;</span>&nbsp;<a href=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/static/journal-watch\">Back to IEEE Journal Watch</a></h3> \n </div> \n <img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/IeeeSpectrumFullText/~4/RV-68mTj0Q8\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" /> \n</link>","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Fri, 14 Aug 2020 13:00:00 +0000","feedId":1936,"bgimg":"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3MjE1MA.jpeg","linkMd5":"56a44e0b7535e96b5d6265ad63e77a1c","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx13/cdn89@2020_4/2020/08/24/14-50-12-951_edfb15cab4889125.webp","destWidth":1240,"destHeight":930,"sourceBytes":136667,"destBytes":66030,"author":"Michelle Hampson","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzI0MTAwOQ.jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx16/cdn17@2020_4/2020/08/24/14-50-13-609_964adaa282b1aac4.webp","https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3NDYxOQ.jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx24/cdn10@2020_2/2020/08/24/14-50-13-510_061910b926b75401.webp","https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3NDYyMA.jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx30/cdn1@2020_3/2020/08/24/14-50-14-635_002a1f8921d5423e.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/IeeeSpectrumFullText/~4/RV-68mTj0Q8":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx8/cdn49@2020_2/2020/08/24/14-50-14-179_96bc0849cfad2709.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598280594949},{"createdTime":"2020-08-24 22:49:54","updatedTime":"2020-08-24 22:49:54","title":"How to Track the Emissions of Every Power Plant on the Planet from Space","link":"https://spectrum.ieee.org/energywise/energy/fossil-fuels/how-to-track-the-emissions-of-every-power-plant-on-the-planet-from-space","description":"<p>Fossil-fuel power plants are one of the largest emitters of the greenhouse gases that cause climate change. Collectively, these 18,000 or so plants account for <a href=\"https://www.wri.org/blog/2020/02/greenhouse-gas-emissions-by-country-sector\">30 percent</a> of global greenhouse gas emissions, including an estimated <a href=\"https://ourworldindata.org/co2-and-other-greenhouse-gas-emissions\">15 billion metric tons</a> of carbon dioxide per year. The pollutants produced by burning fossil fuels also seriously degrade air quality and public health. They contribute to heart and respiratory diseases and lung cancer and are responsible for nearly <a href=\"https://ourworldindata.org/air-pollution\">1 in 10 deaths</a> worldwide.</p> \n<p>Averting the most severe impacts of air pollution and climate change requires understanding the sources of emissions. The technology exists to measure CO<sub>2</sub> and other gases in the atmosphere, but not with enough granularity to pinpoint who emitted what and how much. Last month, a new initiative called <a href=\"https://www.climatetrace.org/\">Climate TRACE</a> was unveiled, with the aim of accurately tracking man-made CO<sub>2</sub> emissions right to the source, no matter where in the world that source is. The coalition of nine organizations and former U.S. Vice President Al Gore has already begun to track such emissions across seven sectors, including electricity, transportation, and forest fires.</p> \n<p>I’m a machine-learning researcher, and in conjunction with the nonprofits <a href=\"https://www.watttime.org/\">WattTime</a>, <a href=\"https://carbontracker.org/\">Carbon Tracker</a>, and the <a href=\"https://www.wri.org/\">World Resources Institute</a> (with funding from <a href=\"https://www.google.org/\">Google.org</a>), I’m working on the electricity piece of Climate TRACE. Using existing satellite imagery and artificial intelligence, we’ll soon be able to estimate emissions from every fossil-fuel power plant in the world. Here’s how we’re doing it.</p> \n<p></p> \n<h2>The current limits of monitoring emissions from space</h2> \n<p>The United States is one of the few countries that publicly releases high-resolution data on emissions from individual power plants. Every major U.S. plant has on-site emissions monitoring equipment and reports data to the <a href=\"https://www.epa.gov/\">Environmental Protection Agency</a>. But the costs of installing and maintaining these systems make them impractical for use in many countries. Monitoring systems can also be <a href=\"https://www.powermag.com/air-emissions-tampering-leads-to-felony-charges-at-power-plant/\">tampered</a> with. Other countries report annual emissions totals that may be rough estimates instead of actual measurements. These estimates lack verification, and they may <a href=\"https://time.com/4099460/china-coal-carbon/\">under-report</a> emissions.</p> \n<p>Greenhouse gas emissions are <a href=\"https://e360.yale.edu/features/paris-conundrum-how-to-know-how-much-carbon-is-being-emitted\">surprisingly difficult</a> to <a href=\"https://www.nature.com/articles/s41558-017-0013-9\">estimate</a>. For one thing, not all of it is man-made. CO<sub>2 </sub>and methane releases from the ocean, volcanoes, decomposition, and soil, plant, and animal respiration also put greenhouse gases into the atmosphere. Then there are the non-obvious man-made contributors such as <a href=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/energy/environment/cement-steel-and-natural-gas-are-major-greenhouse-gas-emitters-too\">cement production</a> and <a href=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/energy/environment/bioengineers-aim-to-break-big-ags-addiction-to-fertilizers\">fertilizers</a>. Even if you know the source, it can be tricky to estimate quantities because the emissions fluctuate. Power plants burning fossil fuels adjust their generation depending on local demand and electricity prices, among other factors.</p> \n<p>Concentrations of CO<sub>2 </sub>are measured locally at observatories such as <a href=\"https://www.esrl.noaa.gov/gmd/ccgg/trends/weekly.html\">Mauna Loa</a>, in Hawaii, and globally by satellites such as NASA’s <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orbiting_Carbon_Observatory_2\">OCO-2</a>. Rather than directly measuring the concentration, satellites estimate it based on how much of the sunlight reflected from Earth is absorbed by carbon dioxide molecules in the air. The European Space Agency’s <a href=\"https://earth.esa.int/web/guest/missions/esa-eo-missions/sentinel-5p\">Sentinel-5P</a> uses similar technology for measuring other greenhouse gases. These spectral measurements are great for creating regional maps of atmospheric CO<sub>2 </sub>concentrations. Such regional estimates have been particularly revealing during the pandemic, as stay-at-home orders led to <a href=\"https://www.iea.org/reports/global-energy-review-2020/global-energy-and-co2-emissions-in-2020\">decreased pollutants</a> reported around cities, largely driven by decreases in <a href=\"https://www.nature.com/articles/s41558-020-0797-x\">transportation</a>.</p> \n<p>But the resolution of these measurements is too low. Each measurement from OCO-2, for example, represents a 1.1-square-mile (2.9-square-kilometer) area on the ground, so it can’t reveal how much an individual power plant emitted (not to mention CO<sub>2 </sub>from natural sources in the area). OCO-2 provides daily observations of each location, but with a great deal of noise due to clouds, wind, and other atmospheric changes. To get a reliable signal and suppress noisy data points, multiple observations of the same site should be averaged over <a href=\"https://atmosphere.copernicus.eu/flawed-estimates-effects-lockdown-measures-air-quality-derived-satellite-observations\">a month</a>.</p> \n<p>To estimate emissions at the source, we need both spatial resolution that’s high enough to see plant operations and frequent observations to see how those measurements change over time.</p> \n<p></p> \n<figure class=\"xlrg\" role=\"img\"> \n <img alt=\"3 satellite images showing visible smoke and water vapor plumes at a power plant.\" src=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3MTk4NA.jpeg\"> \n  <figcaption class=\"hi-cap\">\n    Images: European Space Agency \n  </figcaption> \n  <figcaption>\n    These images from the European Space Agency’s Copernicus Sentinel satellite network show&nbsp;smoke and water vapor plumes when this power plant is operating. \n  </figcaption> </img> \n</figure> \n<h2>How to model power plant emissions with AI</h2> \n<p>We’re fortunate that at any given moment, dozens of satellite networks and hundreds of satellites are capturing the kind of high-resolution imagery we need. Most of these Earth-observing satellites observe in the visible spectrum. We also use thermal infrared to detect heat signatures.</p> \n<p>Having human analysts review images from multiple satellites and cross-referencing them with other data would be too time-consuming, expensive, and error-prone. Our prototype system is starting with data from three satellite networks, from which we collect about 5,000 non-cloudy images per day. The number of images will grow as we incorporate data from additional satellites. Some observations contain information at multiple wavelengths, which means even more data to be analyzed and requiring a finely tuned eye to interpret accurately. No human team could process that much data within a reasonable time frame.</p> \n<p>With AI, the game has changed. Using the same deep-learning approach being applied to speech recognition and to obstacle avoidance in self-driving cars, we’re creating algorithms that lead to much faster prediction of emissions and an enhanced ability to extract patterns from satellite images at multiple wavelengths. The exact patterns the algorithm learns are dependent on the type of satellite and the power plant’s technology.</p> \n<p>We start by matching historical satellite images with plant-reported power generation to create machine-learning models that can learn the relationship between them. Given a novel image of a plant, the model can then predict the plant’s power generation and emissions.</p> \n<p>We have enough ground truth on power generation to train the models. The United States and Taiwan are two of the few countries that report both plant emissions and power generation at hourly intervals. Australia and countries in Europe report generation only, while still other countries report daily aggregated generation. Knowing the power generation and fuel type, we can estimate emissions where that data isn’t reported.</p> \n<p>Once our models have been trained on plants with known power generation, we can apply the models worldwide to any power plant. Our algorithms create predictive models for various satellites and various types of power plants, and we can aggregate the predictions to estimate emissions over a period of time—say, one month.</p> \n<p></p> \n<h2>What our deep-learning models look for in satellite images</h2> \n<p>In a typical fossil-fuel power plant, greenhouse gases&nbsp;exhaust&nbsp;through a chimney called the flue stack, producing a telltale smoke plume that our models can spot. Plants that are more efficient or have secondary collection measures to reduce emissions may have plumes that are difficult to see. In those cases, our models look for other visual and thermal indicators when the power plant’s characteristics are known.</p> \n<p>Another sign the models look for is cooling. Fossil-fuel plants burn fuel to boil water that creates steam to spin&nbsp;a turbine that generates electricity. The steam must then be cooled back into water so that it can be reused to produce more electricity. Depending on the type of cooling technology, a large water vapor plume may be produced from cooling towers, or heat may be released as warm water discharged to a nearby source. We use both visible and thermal imaging to quantify these signals.</p> \n<p></p> \n<h2>Applying our deep-learning models to power plant emissions worldwide</h2> \n<p>So far, we have created and validated an initial set of models for coal-burning plants using generation data from the United States and Europe. Our cross-disciplinary team of scientists and engineers continues to gather and analyze ground-truth data for other countries. As we begin to test our models globally, we will also validate them against reported annual country totals and fuel consumption data. We are starting with CO<sub>2</sub> emissions but hope to expand to other greenhouse gases.</p> \n<p>Our goal is global coverage of fossil-fuel power plant emissions—that is, for any fossil fuel plant in any country, we will be able to accurately predict its emissions of greenhouse gases. Our work for the energy sector is not happening in isolation. <a href=\"https://www.climatetrace.org/\">Climate TRACE</a> grew out of our project on power plants, and it now has a goal to cover 95 percent of man-made greenhouse gas emissions in every sector by mid-2021.</p> \n<p>What comes next? We will make the emissions data public. Renewable energy developers will be able to use it to pinpoint locations where new wind or solar farms will have the most impact. Regulatory agencies will be able to create and enforce new environmental policy. Individual citizens can see how much their local power plants are contributing to climate change. And it may even help track progress toward the <a href=\"https://unfccc.int/process-and-meetings/the-paris-agreement/the-paris-agreement\">Paris Agreement</a> on climate, which is set to be renegotiated in 2021.</p> \n<h2>About the Author</h2> \n<p>Heather D. Couture is the founder of the machine-learning consulting firm <a href=\"http://pixelscientia.com\">Pixel Scientia Labs</a>, which guides R&amp;D teams to fight cancer and climate change more successfully with AI.</p> \n<p></p> \n<img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/IeeeSpectrumFullText/~4/e02bMkx7qeM\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" />","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Tue, 11 Aug 2020 18:00:00 +0000","feedId":1936,"bgimg":"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3MjA1Ng.jpeg","linkMd5":"bdf1ed8e78680838b684fffe333b4ad4","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx21/cdn11@2020_5/2020/08/24/14-50-12-096_93f0d63a420f1daf.webp","destWidth":1240,"destHeight":930,"sourceBytes":107807,"destBytes":107050,"author":"Heather D. Couture","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3MTk4NA.jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx21/cdn37@2020_1/2020/08/24/14-50-14-106_9b8a88bda25afd4d.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/IeeeSpectrumFullText/~4/e02bMkx7qeM":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx21/cdn25@2020_3/2020/08/24/14-50-13-154_88c9312c3e6106be.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598280594950},{"createdTime":"2020-08-24 22:49:54","updatedTime":"2020-08-24 22:49:54","title":"Artificial Antibodies Bolster Hope for Inhaled Coronavirus Treatment","link":"https://spectrum.ieee.org/the-human-os/biomedical/devices/covid19-aeronabs","description":"<link href=\"/ns/interactive/0118race-to-5g/css/5g-logo-treatments.css\" rel=\"stylesheet\"> \n <div class=\"mobileHide\"> \n  <div class=\"imgWrapper offsetLeft lt sm\"> \n   <a href=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/static/covid19-ieee-resources\"><img alt=\"IEEE COVID-19 coverage logo, link to landing page\" src=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzYxMjU3Mg.jpeg\" /></a> \n  </div> \n </div> \n <p>Researchers have developed an inhalable artificial coronavirus antibody that they say “straitjackets” the SARS-CoV-2 virus and appears (in laboratory, albeit still non-clinical, tests) to impede its ability to infect.</p> \n <p>These early tests, they say, suggest the new therapy is potent and powerful enough to merit clinical trials. The ultimate goal, the researchers insist, would be to make it directly available to consumers, possibly even as an over-the-counter home therapy or preventative measure. They say this inhalable COVID prophylaxis/treatment could ideally be ready for public release as soon as “a matter of months.”</p> \n <p>The protein they’ve developed, called mNb6-tri (which they’ve dubbed “AeroNabs”), clamps efficiently and tenaciously atop the spike protein of the SARS-CoV-2 virus, hindering the coronavirus’s ability to infect human cells. And, so far as the researchers have been able to determine, once mNb6-tri locks on to the spike protein, it doesn’t readily come off. That appears to be great news for everyone but the coronavirus.</p> \n <p>Spike proteins are the pointy spines poking out of the coronavirus that make the virus particle look like an inflated blowfish or a porcupine with its back up.</p> \n <p>Spikes—to the world’s great chagrin and horror in 2020—are very good at latching onto a receptor (called ACE2) on many human cells, opening the door for this viral invader. Once inside a cell, the virus hotwires the cell’s machinery to kick out more viral particles, perpetuating a sometimes devastating, body-wide viral infection.</p> \n <p>As it turns out, the antibodies of some mammals (llamas and camels, for instance) consist of simpler proteins called&nbsp;<a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Single-domain_antibody\" target=\"_blank\">nanobodies</a>. And nanobodies can be both an order of magnitude lighter than COVID and relatively easy to characterize and potentially even engineer on an atom-by-atom basis.</p> \n <p>So, if nanobodies were to be pressed into service in the COVID-19 war, the thinking goes, one very potent target could be the invader’s spike protein. That element is such a fundamental component of the coronavirus’s infection protocol that if it were somehow rendered ineffective, the coronavirus’s progress through a human host might be stopped cold, or at least substantially hindered.</p> \n <p>Aashish Manglik, assistant professor of pharmaceutical chemistry at the University of California San Francisco, uses nanobodies extensively in his research and has spent much of this year investigating whether his specialty could help fight the COVID-19 pandemic.</p> \n <p>For this research, <a href=\"https://www.mangliklab.com/\">Manglik’s lab</a> began by performing an experiment in natural selection—that is, they produced a randomized sequence of more than two billion nanobodies.</p> \n <p>“We first created a pool of DNA sequences, where each strand of DNA codes for a unique nanobody,” Manglik says. After that, he recalls: “We put those DNA sequences into individual yeast cells — similar to baker’s yeast, like what we use for at-home cooking. But the cool thing here is we’ve engineered a method where each individual yeast has a unique nanobody tethered to its surface. So in a test tube full of many billions of yeast [cells], each individual yeast will have a different nanobody on its surface.”</p> \n <p>Having done that, the team more or less went fishing, says co-researcher <a href=\"http://walterlab.ucsf.edu/\">Peter Walter</a>, professor of biochemistry and biophysics at UCSF.</p> \n <p>Walter and his research group dropped in a bunch of purified coronavirus spike proteins to the mixture. And because they’d also added a magnetic particle to each spike, when they turned on a magnetic field, they were able to watch as the “fishing lures” rose to the surface with the nanobodies that bound most effectively to the spikes attached.</p> \n <p></p> \n <figure class=\"xlrg\" role=\"img\"> \n  <img alt=\"UCSF graduate student Bryan Faust examines a 3D representation of a COVID-19 spike particle, created using a cryo-electron microscope. \" src=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3NTU0NQ.jpeg\"> \n   <figcaption class=\"hi-cap\">\n     Photo: Noah Berger \n   </figcaption> \n   <figcaption>\n     UCSF graduate student Bryan Faust examines a 3D representation of a COVID-19 spike particle, created using a cryo-electron microscope.&nbsp; \n   </figcaption> </img> \n </figure> \n <p>Some nanobodies held loosely to the spike proteins; others were bound more closely. After a “two- to three-week” whittling process, according to Manglik, the researchers narrowed the pool of two billion candidates down to 20 semi-finalist nanobodies that each appeared to optimize three competing factors. The ideal nanobody would need to: bind quickly and tightly to the spike, remain robust in the presence of heat and other degrading real-world factors, and be as “human-like” as possible. (This last point was necessary to head off any potential complications such as an overzealous human immune system counterproductively attacking the nanobody.)</p> \n <p>Ultimately, they narrowed the nanobody field down to the best of the entire lot: What they called Nb6. Using a new imaging technique called&nbsp;<a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transmission_electron_cryomicroscopy\" target=\"_blank\">cryogenic electron microscopy</a>, they studied Nb6 at the atomic scale as it clamped on to the COVID-19 spike protein. The team made adjustments, they say, that improved Nb6’s binding affinity to the spike by another 500 times.</p> \n <p>Then, because the spike protein is a&nbsp;<a href=\"https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/2170676/\" target=\"_blank\">trimer</a>&nbsp;(containing three identical proteins), the researchers turned their modified nanobody (mNb6) into a tri-fold nanobody specifically engineered to fit the precise contours of the novel coronavirus’s trimer spike. In other words, they'd now created mNb6-tri.</p> \n <p>“This dramatically increases the tightness of binding between this trimeric construct and the spike protein,” says Manglik. “When we did this, we found we got a 200,000-fold gain in the tightness of binding—such that once one of these [AeroNabs nanobodies] binds, it basically never comes off. And we’re at the limit of detection for the kind of instrumentation and the approaches we use to look at how long the molecule, once bound, stays on. We haven’t seen it come off. We think it’ll be bound to that spike protein for weeks if not months.”</p> \n <p>Walter said that because the nanobodies are so small and so effective in seeking out and binding to coronavirus particles, he anticipates that one dose of AeroNabs particles per day could be sufficient for testing its effectiveness for COVID-19 prevention or treatment. Walter calls AeroNabs—if its safety and effectiveness can be substantiated in clinical trials—essentially “molecular PPE.”</p> \n <p>For patients who already have SARS-CoV-2 in their lungs, Walter anticipates a nebulizer creating an AeroNabs mist, which the patient could inhale. “A few minutes once a day should be sufficient,” he said, to test whether AeroNabs could be effective for COVID-19 patients with serious symptoms.</p> \n <p>And because a coronavirus infection often begins in the nasal airways, an AeroNabs nasal spray might also be tested as a preventative measure for front-line workers and other potentially exposed populations as well as people who are positive for the coronavirus but don’t yet show symptoms.</p> \n <p>“AeroNabs is stable and easy to produce in industrial quantities at very low expense,” Walter says. “And it can be shipped around the world as a dry powder. So we really hope it will become broadly available to the developing world as well.”</p> \n <p>On the question of AeroNabs’ safety, Walter and Manglik point to the precedent of another nanobody therapy—<a href=\"https://www.google.com/search?q=Caplacizumab&amp;oq=Caplacizumab&amp;aqs=chrome..69i57j0l7.691j0j4&amp;sourceid=chrome&amp;ie=UTF-8\" target=\"_blank\">Caplacizumab</a>&nbsp;for the blood disorder TTP (<a href=\"https://rarediseases.info.nih.gov/diseases/4607/thrombotic-thrombocytopenic-purpura-acquired\" target=\"_blank\">thrombotic thrombocytopenic purpura</a>0—that was recently approved by the U.S. Food and Drug Administration.</p> \n <p>Walter notes, too, that the name “nanobody” may be deceptive. AeroNabs, he points out, are not in fact nanoparticles or any such exotic creation of nanotechnology. The mNb6-tri protein consists, by design, almost entirely of the same amino acids that make up human proteins. Human airways and bloodstreams are awash in all kinds of proteins that are much like mNb6-tri wherever one might care to look. Only this particular protein has been engineered specifically to bind, as Manglik says, “absurdly tightly” to COVID’s primary tool for breaking and entering human cells.</p> \n <p>For those reasons, the researchers expect toxicity concerns for AeroNabs to be low.</p> \n <p>That said, they’re now seeking commercial partners to take on clinical trials to investigate questions of safety and effectiveness against the coronavirus in human patients in real-world clinical settings.</p> \n <p>The paper describing the group's AeroNabs research was&nbsp;<a href=\"https://www.biorxiv.org/content/10.1101/2020.08.08.238469v1\" target=\"_blank\">published last week</a>&nbsp;on the online pre-print server bioRxiv. Walter says that since then, the group has fielded interest from “quite a number of prestigious journals.”</p> \n <p>“We are all hoping that an effective vaccine with lasting effects can be developed very fast,” Walter said. “But ramping up the production capacity and distribution capacity will take a while. And before that becomes available around the globe, we hope AeroNabs could act as a stopgap measure.”</p> \n <p><iframe allowfullscreen=\"\" frameborder=\"0\" height=\"349\" src=\"//www.youtube.com/embed/WjhbexLtYts?rel=0\" width=\"620\"></iframe></p> \n <div class=\"mobileShow\"> \n  <h3 class=\"RptHdBackBarMobile\"><span class=\"BackArrowBlkBkgrd\">&lt;</span>&nbsp;<a href=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/static/covid19-ieee-resources\">Back to IEEE COVID-19 Resources</a></h3> \n </div> \n <img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/IeeeSpectrumFullText/~4/zQ0P41qxJVI\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" /> \n</link>","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Mon, 17 Aug 2020 20:00:00 +0000","feedId":1936,"bgimg":"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3NTUxMg.jpeg","linkMd5":"4800df969a5c47b0d76aee7ed8cbce29","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx16/cdn93@2020_1/2020/08/24/14-50-11-814_8abab57c3d9fba98.webp","destWidth":1240,"destHeight":930,"sourceBytes":71742,"destBytes":69264,"author":"Mark Anderson","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzYxMjU3Mg.jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx18/cdn17@2020_2/2020/08/24/14-50-14-803_b67305c597a3f2e7.webp","https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3NTU0NQ.jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx10/cdn44@2020_2/2020/08/24/14-50-11-730_b03e55c3806ef0e0.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/IeeeSpectrumFullText/~4/zQ0P41qxJVI":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx1/cdn47@2020_5/2020/08/24/14-50-11-479_d156fcc66bb5638d.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598280594949},{"createdTime":"2020-08-24 22:49:54","updatedTime":"2020-08-24 22:49:54","title":"Building Your Own Cellphone Network Can Be Empowering, and Also Problematic","link":"https://spectrum.ieee.org/telecom/wireless/building-your-own-cellphone-network-can-be-empowering-and-problematic","description":"<figure class=\"xlrg\" role=\"img\"> \n <img alt=\"Scenic photograph of Talea de Castro's antenna in December 2014.\" src=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3MzQ3Mg.jpeg\"> \n  <figcaption class=\"hi-cap\">\n    Photo: Lucas Laursen \n  </figcaption> \n  <figcaption>\n    The antenna for Talea de Castro’s homegrown cellphone network was initiated in March 2013. \n  </figcaption> </img>\n</figure> \n<p>During the summer of 2013, news reports recounted how a Mexican pueblo launched its own do-it-yourself cellphone network. The people of Talea de Castro, population 2,400, created a mini-telecom company, the first of its kind in the world, without help from the government or private companies. They built it after Mexico’s telecommunications giants failed to provide mobile service to the people in the region. It was a fascinating David and Goliath story that pitted the country’s largest corporation against indigenous villagers, many of whom were Zapotec-speaking subsistence farmers with little formal education.</p> \n<p>The reports made a deep impression on me. In the 1990s, I’d spent more than two years in Talea, located in the mountains of Oaxaca in southern Mexico, working as a cultural anthropologist.</p> \n<p>Reading about Talea’s cellular network inspired me to learn about the changes that had swept the pueblo since my last visit. How was it that, for a brief time, the villagers became international celebrities, profiled by <a href=\"https://www.usatoday.com/story/news/world/2013/09/16/mexico-sees-its-first-village-cellphone-network/2821643/\"><em>USA Today</em></a>, <a href=\"https://www.bbc.com/news/world-latin-america-24450542\">BBC News</a>, <a href=\"https://www.wired.com/2015/01/diy-cellular-phone-networks-mexico/\"><em>Wired</em></a>, and many other media outlets? I wanted to find out how the people of this community managed to wire themselves into the 21st century in such an audacious and dramatic way—how, despite their geographic remoteness, they became connected to the rest of the world through the wondrous but unpredictably powerful magic of mobile technology.</p> \n<figure class=\"rt med\" role=\"img\"> \n <img alt=\"Map showing location of Talea de Castro in Mexico.\" src=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3Njc4Mg.jpeg\" style=\"height: 225px; width: 300px;\"> \n  <figcaption class=\"hi-cap\">\n    Illustration: IEEE Spectrum \n  </figcaption> \n  <figcaption>\n    The Oaxacan town of Talea de Castro, population 2,400, is about 120 kilometers by car from Oaxaca City and 580 km from Mexico City. \n  </figcaption> </img>\n</figure> \n<p>I naively thought it would be the Mexican version of a familiar plot line that has driven many Hollywood films: Small-town men and women fearlessly take on big, bad company. Townspeople undergo trials, tribulations, and then…triumph! And they all lived happily ever after.</p> \n<p>I discovered, though, that Talea’s cellphone adventure was much more complicated—neither fairy tale nor cautionary tale. And this latest phase in the pueblo’s centuries-long effort to expand its connectedness to the outside world is still very much a work in progress.</p> \n<p></p> \n<p><strong>Sometimes it’s easy to forget</strong> that 2.5 billion people—one-third of the planet’s population—don’t have cellphones. Many of those living in remote regions want mobile service, but they can’t have it for reasons that have to do with geography, politics, or economics.</p> \n<p>In the early 2000s, a number of Taleans began purchasing cellphones because they traveled frequently to Mexico City or other urban areas to visit relatives or to conduct business. Villagers began petitioning Telcel and Movistar, Mexico’s largest and second-largest wireless operators, for cellular service, and they became increasingly frustrated by the companies’ refusal to consider their requests. Representatives from the firms claimed that it was too expensive to build a mobile network in remote locations—this, in spite of the fact that Telcel’s parent company, América Móvil, is Mexico’s wealthiest corporation. The board of directors is dominated by the Slim family, whose patriarch, Carlos Slim, is among the world’s richest men.</p> \n<p>But in November 2011, an opportunity arose when Talea hosted a three-day conference on indigenous media. Kendra Rodríguez and Abrám Fernández, a married couple who worked at Talea’s community radio station, were among those who attended. Rodríguez is a vivacious and articulate young woman who exudes self-confidence. Fernández, a powerfully built man in his mid-thirties, chooses his words carefully. Rodríguez and Fernández are tech savvy and active on social media. (Note: These names are pseudonyms.)</p> \n<p>Also present were Peter Bloom, a U.S.-born rural-development expert, and Erick Huerta, a Mexican lawyer specializing in telecommunications policy. Bloom had done human rights work in Nigeria in rural areas where “it wasn’t possible to use existing networks because of security concerns and lack of finances,” he explains. “So we began to experiment with software that would enable communication between phones without relying upon any commercial companies.” Huerta had worked with the United Nations’ <a href=\"https://www.itu.int/en/Pages/default.aspx\">International Telecommunication Union</a>.</p> \n<p>The two men founded <a href=\"https://www.rhizomatica.org/\">Rhizomatica</a>, a nonprofit focused on expanding cellphone service in indigenous areas around the world. At the November 2011 conference, they approached Rodríguez and Fernández about creating a community cell network in Talea, and the couple enthusiastically agreed to help.</p> \n<figure class=\"xlrg\" role=\"img\"> \n <img alt=\"Peter Bloom of Rhizomatica shows off the signal strength on a handset outside of Talea de Castro, Mexico. December 2014.\" src=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3MzUzNA.jpeg\"> \n  <figcaption class=\"hi-cap\">\n    Photo: Lucas Laursen \n  </figcaption> \n  <figcaption>\n    Peter Bloom of Rhizomatica shows off the signal strength on a handset outside of Talea de Castro. \n  </figcaption> </img>\n</figure> \n<p>In 2012, Rhizomatica assembled a ragtag team of engineers and hackers to work on the project. They decided to experiment with open-source software called <a href=\"http://openbts.org/\">OpenBTS</a>—Open Base Transceiver Station—which allows cell phones to communicate with each other if they are within range of a base station. Developed by David Burgess and Harvind Samra, cofounders of the San Francisco Bay Area startup <a href=\"https://rangenetworks.com/\">Range Networks</a>, the software also allows networked phones to connect over the Internet using VoIP, or Voice over Internet Protocol. OpenBTS had been successfully deployed at Burning Man and on Niue, a tiny Polynesian island nation.</p> \n<p>Bloom’s team began adapting OpenBTS for Talea. The system requires electrical power and an Internet connection to work. Fortunately, Talea had enjoyed reliable electricity for nearly 40 years and had gotten Internet service in the early 2000s.</p> \n<p>In the meantime, Huerta pulled off a policy coup: He convinced federal regulators that indigenous communities had a legal right to build their own networks. Although the government had granted telecom companies access to nearly the entire radio-frequency spectrum, small portions remained unoccupied. Bloom and his team decided to insert Talea’s network into these unused bands. They would become digital squatters.</p> \n<figure class=\"xlrg\" role=\"img\"> \n <img alt=\"Rhizomatica's coordinator speaks to Talean villagers about cell phone networks.\" src=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3MzQ4MQ.jpeg\"> \n  <figcaption class=\"hi-cap\">\n    Photo:&nbsp;Israel Hernández García \n  </figcaption> \n  <figcaption>\n    A Rhizomatica representative speaks to Talean villagers about the cellphone network. \n  </figcaption> </img>\n</figure> \n<p>As these developments were under way, Rodríguez and Fernández were hard at work informing Taleans about the opportunity that lay before them. Rodríguez used community radio to pique interest among her fellow villagers. Fernández spoke at town hall meetings and coordinated public information sessions at the municipal palace. Soon they had support from hundreds of villagers eager to get connected. Eventually, they voted to invest 400,000 pesos (approximately US $30,000) of municipal funding for equipment to build the cellphone network. By doing so, the village assumed a majority stake in the venture.</p> \n<p></p> \n<p><strong>In March 2013,</strong> the cellphone network was finally ready for a trial run. Volunteers placed an antenna near the town center, in a location they hoped would provide adequate coverage of the mountainside pueblo. As they booted up the system, they looked for the familiar signal bars on their cellphone screens, hoping for a strong reception.</p> \n<p>The bars light up brightly—success!</p> \n<p>The team then walked through the town’s cobblestone streets in order to detect areas with poor reception. As they strolled up and down the paths, they heard laughter and shouting from several houses. People emerged from their homes, stunned.</p> \n<p>“We’ve got service!” exclaimed a woman in disbelief.</p> \n<p>“It’s true, I called the state capital!” cried a neighbor.</p> \n<p>“I just connected to my son in Guadalajara!” shouted another.</p> \n<figure class=\"xlrg\" role=\"img\"> \n <img alt=\"Campesino using a cell phone with Talea visible in the background.\" src=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3MzU1Mw.jpeg\"> \n  <figcaption class=\"hi-cap\">\n    Photo: Agustine Sacha/Rhizomatica \n  </figcaption> \n  <figcaption>\n    A campesino uses his cellphone in the field. Talea is visible in the background. \n  </figcaption> </img>\n</figure> \n<p>Although Rodríguez, Fernández, and Bloom knew that many Taleans had acquired cellphones over the years, they didn’t realize the extent to which the devices were being used every day—not for communications but for listening to music, taking photos and videos, and playing games. Back at the base station, the network computer registered more than 400 cellphones within the antenna’s reach. People were already making calls, and the number was increasing by the minute as word spread throughout town. The team shut down the system to avoid an overload.</p> \n<p>As with any experimental technology, there were glitches. Outlying houses had weak reception. Inclement weather affected service, as did problems with Internet connectivity. Over the next six months, Bloom and his team returned to Talea every week, making the 4-hour trip from Oaxaca City in a red Volkswagen Beetle.</p> \n<p>Their efforts paid off. The community network proved to be immensely popular, and more than 700 people quickly subscribed. The service was remarkably inexpensive: Local calls and text messages were free, and calls to the United States were only 1.5 cents per minute, approximately one-tenth of what it cost using landlines. For example, a 20-minute call to United States from the town’s phone kiosk might cost a campesino a full day’s wages.</p> \n<figure class=\"xlrg\" role=\"img\"> \n <img alt=\"Picture of the equipment enabling mobile communications in Talea de Castro, Oaxaca State, Mexico, taken on August 17, 2013. \" src=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3MzU3MQ.jpeg\"> \n  <figcaption class=\"hi-cap\">\n    Photo: Carlos Salinas/AFP/Getty Images \n  </figcaption> \n  <figcaption>\n    Talea de Castro’s cellphone network relied on open-source software called OpenBTS. \n  </figcaption> </img>\n</figure> \n<p>Initially, the network could handle only 11 phone calls at a time, so villagers voted to impose a 5-minute limit to avoid overloads. Even with these controls, many viewed the network as a resounding success. And patterns of everyday life began to change, practically overnight: Campesinos who walked 2 hours to get to their corn fields could now phone family members if they needed provisions. Elderly women collecting firewood in the forest had a way to call for help in case of injury. Youngsters could send messages to one another without being monitored by their parents, teachers, or peers. And the pueblo’s newest company—a privately owned fleet of three-wheeled <em>mototaxis</em>—increased its business dramatically as prospective passengers used their phones to summon drivers.</p> \n<figure class=\"lt med\" role=\"img\"> \n <img alt=\"Workers assembling a mobile antenna in a Cajonos Zapotec village.\" src=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3MzUyNQ.jpeg\"> \n  <figcaption class=\"hi-cap\">\n    Photo:&nbsp;Israel Hernández García \n  </figcaption> \n  <figcaption>\n    Workers assemble a mobile antenna in a Zapotec village that was inspired by Talea’s network. \n  </figcaption> </img>\n</figure> \n<p>Soon other villages in the region followed Talea’s lead and built their own cellphone networks—first, in nearby Zapotec-speaking pueblos and later, in adjacent communities where Mixe and Mixtec languages are spoken. By late 2015, the villages had formed a cooperative, Telecomunicaciones Indígenas Comunitarias, to help organize the villages’ autonomous networks. Today TIC serves more than 4,000 people in 70 pueblos.</p> \n<p>But some in Talea de Castro began asking questions about management and maintenance of the community network. Who could be trusted with operating this critical innovation upon which villagers had quickly come to depend? What forms of oversight would be needed to safeguard such a vital part of the pueblo’s digital infrastructure? And how would villagers respond if outsiders tried to interfere with the fledgling network?</p> \n<p></p> \n<p><strong>On a chilly morning in May 2014,</strong> Talea’s Monday <em>tianguis</em>, or open-air market, began early and energetically. Some merchants descended upon the mountain village by foot alongside cargo-laden mules. But most arrived on the beds of clattering pickup trucks. By daybreak, dozens of merchants were displaying an astonishing variety of merchandise: produce, fresh meat, live farm animals, eyeglasses, handmade huaraches, electrical appliances, machetes, knockoff Nike sneakers, DVDs, electronic gadgets, and countless other items.</p> \n<p>Hundreds of people from neighboring villages streamed into town on this bright spring morning, and the plaza came alive. By midmorning, the pueblo’s restaurants and cantinas were overflowing. The <em>tianguis </em>provided a welcome opportunity to socialize over coffee or a shot of fiery mezcal.</p> \n<p>As marketgoers bustled through the streets, a voice blared over the speakers of the village’s public address system, inviting people to attend a special event—the introduction of a new, commercial cellphone network. Curious onlookers congregated around the central plaza, drawn by the sounds of a brass band.</p> \n<p>As music filled the air, two young men in polo shirts set up inflatable “sky dancers,” then assembled tables in front of the stately municipal palace. The youths draped the tables with bright blue tablecloths, each imprinted with the logo of Movistar, Mexico’s second-largest wireless provider. Six men then filed out of the municipal palace and sat at the tables, facing the large audience.</p> \n<p>What followed was a ceremony that was part political rally, part corporate event, part advertisement—and part public humiliation. Four of the men were politicians affiliated with the authoritarian Institutional Revolutionary Party, which has dominated Oaxaca state politics for a century. They were accompanied by two Movistar representatives. A pair of young, attractive local women stood nearby, wearing tight jeans and Movistar T-shirts.</p> \n<p>The politicians and company men had journeyed to the pueblo to inaugurate a new commercial cellphone service, called Franquicias Rurales (literally, “Rural Franchises”). As the music faded, one of the politicians launched a verbal attack on Talea’s community network, declaring it to be illegal and fraudulent. Then the Movistar representatives presented their alternative plan.</p> \n<p>A cellphone antenna would soon be installed to provide villagers with Movistar cellphone service—years after a group of Taleans had unsuccessfully petitioned the company to do precisely that. Movistar would rent the antenna to investors, and the franchisees would sell service plans to local customers in turn. Profits would be divided between Movistar and the franchise owners.</p> \n<p>The grand finale was a fiesta, where the visitors gave away cheap cellphones, T-shirts, and other swag as they enrolled villagers in the company’s mobile-phone plans.</p> \n<p></p> \n<figure class=\"xlrg\" role=\"img\"> \n <img alt=\"Talean youth on their cell phones\" src=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3MzU1MQ.jpeg\"> \n  <figcaption class=\"hi-cap\">\n    Photo:&nbsp;Teresa García Bautista \n  </figcaption> \n  <figcaption>\n    Many Talean teens attend high school in Oaxaca City, where they become accustomed to smartphones and the mobile Internet. \n  </figcaption> </img>\n</figure> \n<p><strong>The Movistar event</strong> came at a tough time for the pueblo’s network. From the beginning, it had experienced technical problems. The system’s VoIP technology required a stable Internet connection, which was not always available in Talea. As demand grew, the problems continued, even after a more powerful system manufactured by the Canadian company Nutaq/NuRAN Wireless was installed. Sometimes, too many users saturated the network. Whenever the electricity went out, which is not uncommon during the stormy summer months, technicians would have to painstakingly reboot the system.</p> \n<p>In early 2014, the network encountered a different threat: opposition from the community. Popular support for the network was waning amidst a growing perception that those in charge of maintaining the network were mismanaging the enterprise. Soon, village officials demanded that the network repay the money granted by the municipal treasury. When the municipal government opted to become Talea’s Movistar franchise owner, they used these repaid funds to pay for the company’s antenna. The homegrown network lost more than half of its subscribers in the months following the company’s arrival. In March 2019, it shut down for good.</p> \n<figure class=\"rt med\" role=\"img\"> \n <img alt=\"Movistar store\" src=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3MzUxNQ.jpeg\"> \n  <figcaption class=\"hi-cap\">\n    Photo:&nbsp;Teresa García Bautista \n  </figcaption> \n  <figcaption> \n   <p>When Movistar began offering cellphone service in Talea, customers flocked to it. The homegrown network shut down in March 2019.</p> \n  </figcaption> </img>\n</figure> \n<p>No one forced Taleans to turn their backs on the autonomous cell network that had brought them international fame. There were many reasons behind the pueblo’s turn to Movistar. Although the politicians who condemned the community network were partly responsible, the cachet of a globally recognized brand also helped Movistar succeed. The most widely viewed television events in the pueblo (as in much of the rest of the world) are World Cup soccer matches. As Movistar employees were signing Taleans up for service during the summer of 2014, the Mexican national soccer team could be seen on TV wearing jerseys that prominently featured the telecom company’s logo.</p> \n<p>But perhaps most importantly, young villagers were drawn to Movistar because of a burning desire for mobile data services. Many teenagers from Talea attended high school in the state capital, Oaxaca City, where they became accustomed to the Internet and all that it offers.</p> \n<p>Was Movistar’s goal to smash an incipient system of community-controlled communication, destroy a bold vision for an alternative future, and prove that villagers are incapable of managing affairs themselves? It’s hard to say. The company’s goals were probably motivated more by long-term financial objectives: to increase market share by extending its reach into remote regions.</p> \n<p>And yet, despite the demise of Talea’s homegrown network, the idea of community-based telecoms had taken hold. The pueblo had paved the way for significant legal and regulatory changes that made it possible to create similar networks in nearby communities, which continue to thrive.</p> \n<figure class=\"rt med-sm\" role=\"img\"> \n <img alt=\"Cover of the book Connected: How a Mexican Village Built Its Own Cell Phone Network \" src=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3MzUwNg.jpeg\"> \n  <figcaption class=\"hi-cap\">\n    Image: University of California Press \n  </figcaption> </img>\n</figure> \n<p>The people of Talea have long been open to outside ideas and technologies, and have maintained a pragmatic attitude and a willingness to innovate. The same outlook that led villagers to enthusiastically embrace the foreign hackers from Rhizomatica also led them to give Movistar a chance to redeem itself after having ignored the pueblo’s earlier appeals. In this town, people greatly value convenience and communication. As I tell students at my university, located in the heart of Silicon Valley, villagers for many years have longed to be reliably and conveniently connected to each other—and to the rest of the world. In other words, Taleans want cellphones because they want to be like you.</p> \n<p></p> \n<p><em>This article is based on excerpts from </em><a href=\"https://www.ucpress.edu/book/9780520344211/connected\"><em>Connected: How a Mexican Village Built Its Own Cell Phone Network</em></a> (University of California Press, 2020).</p> \n<p></p> \n<p></p> \n<h2>About the Author</h2> \n<p><a href=\"https://www.sjsu.edu/anthropology/people/permanent/gonzalez/\">Roberto J. González</a> is chair of the anthropology department at San José State University.</p> \n<p></p> \n<p></p> \n<p></p>\n<img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/IeeeSpectrumFullText/~4/uVYezt4zuL0\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" />","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Wed, 19 Aug 2020 21:30:00 +0000","feedId":1936,"bgimg":"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3MzU4MA.jpeg","linkMd5":"83e4bcae28a453a6bed712c13ed949ae","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx30/cdn67@2020_4/2020/08/24/14-50-11-821_cbcc768e3b084f29.webp","destWidth":1240,"destHeight":930,"sourceBytes":170213,"destBytes":189018,"author":"Roberto J. González","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3MzQ3Mg.jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx/cdn51@2020_2/2020/08/24/14-50-11-878_25e9aeccc9c8ffc8.webp","https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3Njc4Mg.jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx18/cdn97@2020_5/2020/08/24/14-50-13-010_b3c36206b502e18e.webp","https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3MzUzNA.jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx27/cdn69@2020_6/2020/08/24/14-50-13-226_833bf53cea0012fb.webp","https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3MzQ4MQ.jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx22/cdn66@2020_4/2020/08/24/14-50-14-318_00bc5811327076ec.webp","https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3MzU1Mw.jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx22/cdn40@2020_1/2020/08/24/14-50-11-799_f45fc86649c99dc0.webp","https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3MzU3MQ.jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx10/cdn70@2020_5/2020/08/24/14-50-14-336_ac566dc94a91897f.webp","https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3MzUyNQ.jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx14/cdn90@2020_4/2020/08/24/14-50-13-508_be3deb3f5b275d83.webp","https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3MzU1MQ.jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx19/cdn74@2020_6/2020/08/24/14-50-13-431_cb35e6cd79534a2c.webp","https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3MzUxNQ.jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx6/cdn6@2020_2/2020/08/24/14-50-14-339_5d0e70818a6ea81b.webp","https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3MzUwNg.jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn53@2020_1/2020/08/24/14-50-14-272_23fc4a6c9f9690e1.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/IeeeSpectrumFullText/~4/uVYezt4zuL0":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx2/cdn62@2020_2/2020/08/24/14-50-14-099_8b3423039cb6b9a9.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598280594947},{"createdTime":"2020-08-24 22:49:54","updatedTime":"2020-08-24 22:49:54","title":"The Electric Weed-Zapper Renaissance","link":"https://spectrum.ieee.org/tech-talk/energy/environment/the-electric-weed-zapper-renaissance","description":"<p>In the 1890s, U.S. railroad companies struggled with what remains a problem for railroads across the world: weeds. The solution that 19th-century railroad engineers devised made use of a then-new technology—high-voltage electricity, which <a href=\"https://patents.google.com/patent/US546682A/en\">they discovered</a> could zap troublesome vegetation overgrowing their tracks. Somewhat later, the people in charge of maintaining tracks turned to using fire instead. But the approach to weed control that they and countless others ultimately adopted was&nbsp;applying chemical herbicides, which were easier to manage and more effective.</p> \n<p>The use of herbicides, whether on railroad rights of way, agricultural fields, or suburban gardens, later raised health concerns, though. More than 100,000 people in the United States, for example, have claimed that Monsanto’s Roundup weed killer caused them to get cancer—claims that Bayer, which now owns Monsanto, is <a href=\"https://www.nytimes.com/2020/06/24/business/roundup-settlement-lawsuits.html\">trying hard of late to settle</a>.</p> \n<p>Meanwhile, more and more places are banning the use of Roundup and similar glyphosate herbicides. Currently, half of all U.S. states have <a href=\"https://www.carlsonattorneys.com/news-and-update/banning-roundup\" style=\"font-family: Georgia, serif; font-size: 18px;\">legal restrictions</a>&nbsp;in place&nbsp;that limit the use of such chemical weed killers. Such restrictions are also in place in 19 other countries, including Austria, which banned the chemical in 2019, and Germany, which will be phasing it out by 2023. So, it’s no wonder that the concept of using electricity to kill weeds is undergoing a renaissance.</p> \n<p>Actually, the idea never really died. A U.S. company called Lasco has been selling <a href=\"https://www.lightningweeder.com/\">electric weed-killing equipment</a> for decades. More recently, another U.S. company has been marketing this technology under the name “<a href=\"https://theweedzapper.com/\">The Weed Zapper</a>.” But the most interesting developments along these lines are in Europe, where electric weed control seems to be gaining real traction.</p> \n<p>One company trying to replace herbicides with electricity is <a href=\"http://rootwave.com/\">RootWave</a>, based in the U.K. Andrew Diprose, RootWave’s CEO, is the son of Michael Diprose, who spent much of his career as a researcher at the <a href=\"https://www.sheffield.ac.uk/\">University of Sheffield</a> studying ways to control weeds with electricity.</p> \n<p>Electricity, the younger Diprose explains, boasts some key benefits over other non-chemical forms of weed control, which include using hot water, steam, and mechanical extraction. In particular, electric weed control doesn’t require any water. It’s also considerably more energy efficient than using steam, which requires an order of magnitude more fuel. And unlike mechanical means, electric weed killing is also consistent with modern “<a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/No-till_farming#:~:text=No%2Dtill%20farming%20(also%20known,dry%20soils%20on%20sloping%20terrain.\">no till</a>” agricultural practices. What’s more, Diprose asserts, the cost is now comparable with chemical herbicides.</p> \n<p>Unlike the electric weed-killing gear that’s long been sold&nbsp;in the United States, RootWave’s equipment runs at <span>tens of kilohertz—</span>a much higher frequency than the power mains. This brings two advantages. For one, it makes the equipment lighter, because the transformers required to raise the voltage to weed-zapping levels (thousands of volts) can be much smaller. It also makes the equipment safer, because higher frequencies pose less of a threat of electrocution. Should you accidentally touch a live electrode “you will get a burn,” says Diprose, but there is much less of a threat of causing cardiac arrest than there would be with a system that operated at 50 or 60 hertz.</p> \n<p>RootWave has two systems, a hand-carried one operating at 5 kilowatts and a 20-kilowatt version carried by a tractor. The company is currently collaborating with various industrial partners, including another U.K. startup called <a href=\"https://www.smallrobotcompany.com/\">Small Robot Company</a>, which plans to outfit an agricultural robot for automated weed killing with electricity.&nbsp;</p> \n<p>And RootWave isn’t the only European company trying to revive this old idea. Netherlands-based <a href=\"https://www.cnhindustrial.com/en-us/Pages/homepage.aspx\">CNH Industrial</a>&nbsp;is also promoting electric weed control with a tractor-mounted system it has dubbed “<a href=\"https://agxtend.com/products/xpower\">XPower</a>.” Like RootWave’s tractor-mounted system, the electrodes are swept over a field at&nbsp;a prescribed height, killing the weeds that poke up higher than the crop to be preserved.</p> \n<p>Of the many advantages CNH touts for its weed-electrocution system (which presumably applies to all such systems, ever since the 1890s) is “No specific resistance expectable.” I should certainly hope not. But I do think that a more apropos wording here, for something that destroys weeds by placing them in a high-voltage electrical circuit, might be a phrase that both <em>Star Trek</em> fans and electrical engineers could better appreciate: “Resistance is futile.</p>\n<img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/IeeeSpectrumFullText/~4/KPnYQ3a2qrw\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" />","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Tue, 18 Aug 2020 18:30:00 +0000","feedId":1936,"bgimg":"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3Njg4Mg.jpeg","linkMd5":"70d8920bb4179fd71e12cb38b136c01e","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx25/cdn19@2020_1/2020/08/24/14-50-11-851_1146d3ac8896d351.webp","destWidth":1240,"destHeight":930,"sourceBytes":142651,"destBytes":159340,"author":"David Schneider","articleImgCdnMap":{"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/IeeeSpectrumFullText/~4/KPnYQ3a2qrw":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx11/cdn10@2020_3/2020/08/24/14-50-13-024_573d992476016271.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598280594948},{"createdTime":"2020-08-24 22:49:54","updatedTime":"2020-08-24 22:49:54","title":"ALOHAnet Introduced Random Access Protocols to the Computing World","link":"https://spectrum.ieee.org/the-institute/ieee-history/alohanet-introduced-random-access-protocols-to-the-computing-world","description":"<style type=\"text/css\">.entry-content .tisubhead { color: #999999; font-family: verdana; font-size: 14px; font-weight: bold; letter-spacing: 1px; margin-bottom: -5px !important; text-transform: uppercase; } .tiopener { color: #0f4994; font-family: Theinhardt-Medium, sans-serif; letter-spacing: 1px; margin-right: 10px; font-weight: bold; text-transform: uppercase; } </style> \n<figure class=\"xlrg\" role=\"img\"> \n <img alt=\" A woman sits in front of an AlohaNET computer in 1971.\" src=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3MzI2Mw.jpeg\"> \n  <div class=\"ai\"> \n   <figcaption class=\"hi-cap\">\n     Photo: Norm Abramson/University of Hawaiʻi at Mānoa \n   </figcaption> \n   <figcaption>\n     This user is operating the ALOHAnet hub machine, which&nbsp;used a random access protocol to transmit packets over a shared channel. \n   </figcaption> \n  </div> </img> \n</figure> \n<p><span class=\"tiopener\">THE INSTITUTE </span>Until the 1970s, far-flung computers generally connected to one another through telephone networks. In 1968 researchers at the <a href=\"https://www.hawaii.edu/\">University of Hawaii</a> began to investigate if radio communications could be used to link multiple computers at once.</p> \n<p>The team introduced its Additive Links On-line Hawaii Area network, ALOHAnet, in June 1971. The network used a random access protocol, which allowed computers to transmit packets over a shared channel, as soon as they had information to send. ALOHAnet was the first use of wireless communications for a data network. Its protocol is now widely used in nearly all forms of wireless communications.</p> \n<p>“We [the team] thought that what we were doing would be important, but I don’t think any of us thought it would be as important as it turned out to be,” IEEE Life Fellow <a href=\"http://www-ee.eng.hawaii.edu/faculty/detail.php?usr=34\">Norman Abramson</a>, who led the team, said in a <a href=\"http://www2.hawaii.edu/~pager/Aloha%20History.pdf\">2009 interview</a> about ALOHAnet in <em>IEEE Communications Magazine</em>. “It exceeded my wildest expectations.”</p> \n<p>ALOHAnet is now an IEEE Milestone. Its nomination was sponsored by the IEEE Hawaii Section. Administered by the <a href=\"http://www.ieee.org/about/history_center/index.html\">IEEE History Center</a> and <a href=\"https://www.ieeefoundation.org/donate_history\">supported by donors</a>, the Milestone program recognizes outstanding technical developments around the world.</p> \n<p>The dedication ceremony, originally planned for June 2020 at the University of Hawaii at Manoa, in Honolulu, was postponed until next year due to the <a href=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/tag/coronavirus?utm_source=spectrum-hero\">COVID-19 pandemic</a>.</p> \n<h3 class=\"tisubhead\">ESTABLISHING A CONNECTION</h3> \n<p>The University of Hawaii used ALOHAnet to connect its campuses to one another. Each campus had a small interface computer—a hub machine—that used two distinct radio frequencies: an outbound channel and an inbound channel. In order to connect, one hub machine broadcasted packets to another computer on the outbound channel, and that computer sent data packets to the first hub machine on the inbound channel.</p> \n<p>If data was successfully received at the hub, a short acknowledgment packet was sent back. If an acknowledgment was not received by the computer, it would automatically retransmit the data packet after waiting for a randomly selected amount of time. The mechanism detected and corrected collisions that were created when the machine and the computer attempted to send a packet at the same time, according to the <a href=\"http://ieeemilestones.ethw.org/Milestone-Proposal:ALOHANET_(aka_ALOHA_System)\">Engineering and Technology History Wiki entry</a> about the Milestone.</p> \n<p>Computer networks were not well understood at the time, and it took several years for the researchers to perfect their design.</p> \n<p>“In a sense, [the acknowledgement mechanism is] an obvious thing to do,” Abramson said in the article. “But when you start off on this kind of research project, some of the obvious things don’t appear as obvious as they do a little later.”</p> \n<p>ALOHAnet was connected to <a href=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/tech-talk/tech-history/dawn-of-electronics/untold-history-of-ai-darpa-dream-of-cyborg-intelligence\">ARPANET</a> via satellite in December 1972 under the guidance of the <a href=\"https://www.darpa.mil/\">U.S.&nbsp;Defense Advanced Research Projects Agency</a>. The connection allowed for reliable computer communications throughout the United States, according to the Wiki entry.</p> \n<p>ALOHAnet used a VHF transponder in 1973 to connect to an experimental NASA satellite in order to demonstrate <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pacnet\">PacNet</a>, an international satellite data network. The demonstration connected the NASA facility in California with five universities in Australia, Japan, and the United States, the Wiki entry says.</p> \n<p>The Milestone plaque is to be displayed at the entrance of Holmes Hall at the University of Hawaii at Manoa, which was where the technology was developed, tested, and demonstrated. The plaque reads:</p> \n<p><em>In June 1971 the ALOHA packet radio data network began providing inter-island access to computing facilities at the University of Hawaii. ALOHAnet was the first to demonstrate that communication channels could be effectively and efficiently shared on a large scale using simple random access protocols. It led directly to the development of Ethernet and personal wireless communication technologies.</em></p> \n<p>This article was written with assistance from the IEEE History Center, which is funded by donations to the <a href=\"https://www.ieeefoundation.org/\">IEEE Foundation</a>’s <a href=\"https://www.ieeefoundation.org/campaign\">Realize the Full Potential of IEEE campaign</a>.</p> \n<img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/IeeeSpectrumFullText/~4/K9No9v17AjI\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" />","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Wed, 12 Aug 2020 18:00:00 +0000","feedId":1936,"bgimg":"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3MzI2NA.jpeg","linkMd5":"93ebe81774e46c24e8036a4d6a0c9c48","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx2/cdn22@2020_2/2020/08/24/14-50-12-541_1a2931ab78f492ff.webp","destWidth":1240,"destHeight":930,"sourceBytes":86971,"destBytes":80334,"author":"Joanna Goodrich","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3MzI2Mw.jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx33/cdn13@2020_3/2020/08/24/14-50-13-500_40d0984ee5988f5d.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/IeeeSpectrumFullText/~4/K9No9v17AjI":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx31/cdn14@2020_2/2020/08/24/14-50-15-978_dc9e38b741e58cb4.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598280594949},{"createdTime":"2020-08-24 22:49:54","updatedTime":"2020-08-24 22:49:54","title":"IBM Computer Scientist Frances E. Allen, Who Advanced Modern Computing, Dies at 88","link":"https://spectrum.ieee.org/the-institute/ieee-member-news/ibm-computer-scientist-frances-e-allen-who-advanced-modern-computing-dies-at-88","description":"<style type=\"text/css\">.entry-content .tisubhead { color: #999999; font-family: verdana; font-size: 14px; font-weight: bold; letter-spacing: 1px; margin-bottom: -5px !important; text-transform: uppercase; } .tiopener { color: #0f4994; font-family: Theinhardt-Medium, sans-serif; letter-spacing: 1px; margin-right: 10px; font-weight: bold; text-transform: uppercase; } </style> \n<figure class=\"xlrg\" role=\"img\"> \n <img alt=\"Photograph of Frances E. Allen\" src=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY2MDA5NQ.jpeg\"> \n  <figcaption class=\"hi-cap\">\n    Photo: IBM Research \n  </figcaption> </img> \n</figure> \n<p><span class=\"tiopener\">THE INSTITUTE </span>Computing pioneer <a href=\"https://www.computer.org/profiles/frances-allen\">Frances E. Allen</a>, an IEEE Fellow, died on 4 August, the day of her 88th birthday.</p> \n<p>Earlier this year, IEEE established <a href=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/the-institute/ieee-news/new-ieee-medal-named-for-computing-pioneer-frances-e-allen\">a medal</a><span class=\"MsoHyperlink\"> in her honor. Sponsored by <a href=\"https://www.draco.res.ibm.com/\">IBM</a>, the Allen Medal recognizes innovative work in computing that led to a lasting impact on the field of engineering, technology, or science.</span></p> \n<p>“Fran will be remembered as a pioneer in the world of computing who made seminal contributions to the field of optimizing compilers,” said Dario Gil, the director of <a href=\"https://www.draco.res.ibm.com/\">IBM</a><span class=\"MsoHyperlink\"> Research, in a statement published by <a href=\"https://www.washingtonpost.com/local/obituaries/frances-allen-first-woman-to-win-turing-award-for-contributions-to-computing-dies-at-88/2020/08/06/7ea7d7a2-d7f0-11ea-930e-d88518c57dcc_story.html\"><em>The Washington Post</em></a>. “She left an enduring mark on IBM and will be remembered not only for her technical vision and legacy, but also her passion to inspire and help others, especially women.”</span></p> \n<p>Allen was the first woman to receive the <a href=\"https://www.acm.org/about-acm/about-the-acm-organization\">Association for Computing Machinery</a>’s <a href=\"https://amturing.acm.org/\">A.M. Turing Award</a> in 2006 for her contributions to computing.</p> \n<h3 class=\"tisubhead\">ACCIDENTAL ENGINEER</h3> \n<p>Allen grew up on a farm in Peru, N.Y. She was inspired to teach mathematics by one of her high school teachers, according to a 2002 <a href=\"https://ethw.org/Oral-History:Frances_%22Fran%22_Allen\">oral history conducted</a> by the <a href=\"https://www.ieee.org/about/history-center/index.html\">IEEE History Center</a>.</p> \n<p>She went on to receive a bachelor’s degree in mathematics in 1954 from the New York State College for Teachers, now the <a href=\"https://www.albany.edu/\">State University of New York at Albany</a>. After graduating, Allen taught math at the same high school from which she had graduated.</p> \n<p>After two years of teaching, she decided to pursue a master’s degree at the <a href=\"https://umich.edu/\">University of Michigan</a> in Ann Arbor. While there, she took a handful of basic computing classes and learned how to program an IBM 650 data-processing machine, according to her <a href=\"https://amturing.acm.org/award_winners/allen_1012327.cfm\">biography on the Turing Award website</a>.</p> \n<p>IBM recruited Allen and offered her a position in the company’s research division, in Poughkeepsie, N.Y. After graduating from the University of Michigan in 1957, she began teaching the company’s researchers Fortran, the first high-level programming language. She planned to work at the company until she paid off her student loans, but she ended up staying at IBM for her entire 45-year career, retiring in 2002.</p> \n<p>She was the first woman to be named an IBM Fellow, the highest honor the company bestows on scientists, engineers, and programmers.</p> \n<p>During her career, she developed a number of cutting-edge programming-language compilers.</p> \n<p>In the early 1960s she led a team of researchers that designed one of the first supercomputers—the <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IBM_7950_Harvest\">Stretch Harvest</a>—for the U.S. <a href=\"https://www.nsa.gov/\">National Security Agency</a>. The machine could decrypt messages using three different programming languages: Alpha, Autocoder, and Fortran.</p> \n<p>She designed and built the machine-independent, language-independent optimizing component of the Experimental Compiler for <a href=\"https://computerhistory.org/events/ibm-acs-system-pioneering-supercomputer/\">IBM’s Advanced Computing Systems</a>. The code helped drive technological improvements of hardware design, and it created a new way to analyze and transform programs.</p> \n<p>Allen wrote a seminal paper, “Program Optimization,” first published internally at IBM in 1966. It describes a robust new framework for implementing program analysis and optimization as well as a powerful set of new algorithms. “A Catalog of Optimizing Transformations”—a paper she wrote with <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Cocke\">John Cocke</a>, a computer scientist who is considered to be the “father of RISC architecture,” that was published in 1972—identified many transformations commonly used today.</p> \n<p>Allen took great pride in mentoring new IBM employees, especially women who joined the organization, according to the 2002 oral history.</p> \n<p>When “people come to an organization, particularly if they’ve just come out of school, they suddenly find themselves in a very alien environment,” Allen said in 2002. “The culture is different, and how to succeed is very puzzling, and there’s a management system and a performance evaluation system. I had started to try and make myself a focal point for women who were entering our organization. I could try and help them build networks.”</p> \n<p>In 2000 IBM established the Frances E. Allen Women in Technology Mentoring Award.</p> \n<p>Allen was a member of the <a href=\"https://www.amacad.org/\">American Academy of Arts and Sciences</a>, the <a href=\"https://www.amphilsoc.org/\">American Philosophical Society</a>, and the <a href=\"https://www.nae.edu/\">National Academy of Engineering</a>. She was inducted into the <a href=\"https://www.witi.com/halloffame/\">Women in Technology Hall of Fame</a> in 1997 and received the 2002 <a href=\"https://www.awc-hq.org/ada-lovelace-awards.html\">Lovelace Award</a> from the <a href=\"https://www.awc-hq.org/home.html\">Association for Women in Computing</a>.</p> \n<p>In her free time, Allen was an avid mountain climber and studied environmental issues, according to her <a href=\"https://www.ibm.com/blogs/research/2020/08/remembering-frances-allen/\">obituary on IBM’s Research Blog</a>. She was a member of the <a href=\"https://americanalpineclub.org/\">American Alpine Club</a> and the <a href=\"https://www.alpineclubofcanada.ca/web/\">Alpine Club of Canada</a>.</p> \n<img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/IeeeSpectrumFullText/~4/Pzk5f0yeCMA\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" />","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Fri, 21 Aug 2020 18:30:00 +0000","feedId":1936,"bgimg":"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3ODgxNg.jpeg","linkMd5":"02284c15213f4bb593d6ece50a2dab99","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx1/cdn35@2020_1/2020/08/24/14-50-13-793_96c1c666aeb06880.webp","destWidth":1240,"destHeight":746,"sourceBytes":56579,"destBytes":54412,"author":"Joanna Goodrich","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY2MDA5NQ.jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx25/cdn45@2020_3/2020/08/24/14-50-13-244_cd4380a87e811492.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/IeeeSpectrumFullText/~4/Pzk5f0yeCMA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx6/cdn86@2020_4/2020/08/24/14-50-12-583_c3a88bf8669edf02.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598280594946},{"createdTime":"2020-08-24 22:49:54","updatedTime":"2020-08-24 22:49:54","title":"Amazon’s Project Kuiper is More Than the Company’s Response to SpaceX","link":"https://spectrum.ieee.org/tech-talk/aerospace/satellites/amazons-project-kuiper-is-more-than-the-companys-response-to-spacex","description":"<p><a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/\">Amazon</a> cleared an important hurdle when the U.S.&nbsp;<a href=\"https://www.fcc.gov/\">Federal Communications Commission</a> (FCC) announced on 30 July&nbsp;that <a href=\"https://www.fcc.gov/document/fcc-authorizes-kuiper-satellite-constellation\">the company was authorized</a> to deploy and operate its <a href=\"https://blog.aboutamazon.com/company-news/amazon-receives-fcc-approval-for-project-kuiper-satellite-constellation\">Kuiper satellite constellation</a>. The authorization came with the caveat that Amazon would still have to demonstrate that Kuiper would not interfere with previously authorized satellite projects, such as <a href=\"https://www.spacex.com/\">SpaceX</a>’s Starlink.</p> \n<p></p> \n<p>Even with the FCC’s caveat, it’s tempting to imagine that the idea of putting a mega-constellation of thousands of satellites in low-Earth orbit to provide uninterrupted broadband access anywhere on Earth will become a battle between Jeff Bezos’ Kuiper and Elon Musk’s <a href=\"https://www.starlink.com/\">Starlink</a>. After all, even in space, how much room can there be for two mega-constellations, let alone additional efforts like that of the <a href=\"https://www.forbes.com/sites/jonathanocallaghan/2020/07/03/uk-government-wins-controversial-bid-for-bankrupt-mega-constellation-firm-oneweb/#1a751f075b9d\">recently-beleaguered OneWeb</a>?
