Thus, it is important to design robots to be safe when people act adversarially towards them, both from a physical and computational perspective.&nbsp;</li> \n <li>In terms of how robots should react to mistreatment, our past work suggests that it is better to have the robot express sadness and shutdown for a few seconds than to make it react in a more emotional manner or not react at all. The shutdown strategy was also effective in our latest experiment.&nbsp;</li> \n <li>It is possible for robots to leverage their social context to reduce the effect of adversarial actions towards them. For example, they can motivate bystanders to intervene or help, as shown in our latest study.&nbsp;</li> \n</ol> \n<p><strong>What are you working on next?</strong></p> \n<p>We are working on better understanding the different reasons that motivated prosocial interventions in our study: subconscious empathy, group dynamics, and conformity by omission. We are also working towards creating a social robot at Yale that we can easily deploy in public locations such that we can study group human-robot interactions in more realistic and unconstrained settings. Our work on robot abuse has informed several aspects of the design of this public robot. We look forward to testing the platform after our campus activities, which are on hold due to COVID-19, resume back to normal.</p> \n<hr> \n <p>“Prompting Prosocial Human Interventions in Response to Robot Mistreatment,” by Joe Connolly, Viola Mocz, Nicole Salomons, Joseph Valdez, Nathan Tsoi, Brian Scassellati, and Marynel Vázquez from Yale University, was presented at HRI 2020.&nbsp;</p> \n <img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/IeeeSpectrumFullText/~4/xcdY226yfWU\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" /> \n</hr>","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Wed, 19 Aug 2020 13:00:00 +0000","feedId":1936,"bgimg":"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3NzE3Mw.jpeg","linkMd5":"e98b4d49e2b78a9b0a4c6a1e5990530c","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx15/cdn59@2020_4/2020/08/24/14-50-11-681_74df73fac80d8097.webp","destWidth":1240,"destHeight":1000,"sourceBytes":305065,"destBytes":64314,"author":"Evan Ackerman","articleImgCdnMap":{"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/IeeeSpectrumFullText/~4/xcdY226yfWU":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx23/cdn18@2020_5/2020/08/24/14-50-13-076_83733fa17eccb93d.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598280594948},{"createdTime":"2020-08-24 22:49:54","updatedTime":"2020-08-24 22:49:54","title":"New LiDAR Sensor Uses Mirrors to Achieve High Efficiency","link":"https://spectrum.ieee.org/tech-talk/consumer-electronics/gadgets/new-lidar-sensor-uses-mirrors-to-achieve-high-efficiency","description":"<link href=\"/ns/interactive/0118race-to-5g/css/5g-logo-treatments.css\" rel=\"stylesheet\"> \n <div class=\"mobileHide\"> \n  <div class=\"imgWrapper offsetLeft lt sm\"> \n   <a href=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/static/journal-watch\"><img alt=\"Journal Watch report logo, link to report landing page\" src=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzI0MTAwOQ.jpeg\" /></a> \n  </div> \n </div> \n <p>Light Detection and Ranging (LiDAR) technology relies on fast, precisely timed laser pulses – a useful application for various kinds of sensors, including those that support the Internet of Things (IoT). However, many current sensors that rely on LiDAR are expensive, bulky, heavy, and power hungry.</p> \n <p>One group of researchers is proposing a novel design for a LiDAR-based sensor that is both affordable and requires very little power. The sensor relies on a collection of microelectromechanical (MEMS) mirrors to achieve high efficiency – enough so to be powered by a 9-volt battery. The design is described in a <a href=\"https://ieeexplore.ieee.org/document/9131806\">study</a> published 2 July in <a href=\"https://ieeexplore.ieee.org/xpl/mostRecentIssue.jsp?punumber=7782634\"><em>IEEE Sensors Letters</em></a>.</p> \n <p>“This system is especially suitable for smart buildings,” says Huikai Xie, a professor at the University of Florida who co-designed the new LiDAR-based sensor. “For instance, this system may enable heating, ventilation, and air-conditioning (HVAC) systems to control air flow by estimating the population distribution and tracking people’s movement.”</p> \n <p>An outstanding issue, however, is creating LiDAR sensors that can achieve a wide field of view without consuming too much energy. Existing designs tend to rely on motorized optomechanical scanners to disperse the LiDAR signals and achieve a wider field of view—yet these devices typically consume about 10 watts of power.</p> \n <p></p> \n <figure class=\"xlrg\" role=\"img\"> \n  <img alt=\"Low-power MEMS LiDAR in a portable enclosure\" src=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3MDM1OQ.jpeg\"> \n   <figcaption class=\"hi-cap\">\n     Image: Dingkang Wang \n   </figcaption> \n   <figcaption>\n     Low-power MEMS LiDAR in a portable enclosure \n   </figcaption> </img> \n </figure> \n <p><span>Instead of a motorized optomechanical scanner, the new design by Xie’s group relies on MEMS mirrors to control the LiDAR signals. The mirrors require significantly less power to manipulate than the bulkier motorized scanner that have typically been used. What’s more, a passive infrared sensor ensures that the whole system is only activated when people are present.</span><br> <span>In the future, Xie says he envisions this sensor being used not just for detecting people in smart homes,&nbsp; but for applications ranging from robotics to&nbsp; small unmanned air vehicles.</span></br></p> \n <p><span>The design currently relies on an off-the-shelf time-of-flight (TOF) engine for analyzing the returning laser signals, which Xie says is the bulkiest and most energy-intensive component. Moving forward, his team plans on developing their own, smaller (TOF) device that uses less energy. As well, he says, “We will continue advancing the MEMS technology by making MEMS mirrors with larger optical aperture, larger scan angle and faster scan frequency.”</span></p> \n <p>“The whole MEMS LiDAR system can be powered by a battery with a maximum power consumption of 2.7 W,” says Xie. “The passive infrared sensor can put the system into an idle mode, which extends the battery life by three times or more.”</p> \n <p>In the future, Xie says he envisions this sensor being used not just for detecting people in smart homes, &nbsp;but for applications ranging from robotics to &nbsp;small unmanned air vehicles.</p> \n <p><em>This article appears in the September 2020 print issue as “Lidar Sensor Uses Mirrors to Amp Up Efficiency.”</em></p> \n <div class=\"mobileShow\"> \n  <h3 class=\"RptHdBackBarMobile\"><span class=\"BackArrowBlkBkgrd\">&lt;</span>&nbsp;<a href=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/static/journal-watch\">Back to IEEE Journal Watch</a></h3> \n </div> \n <img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/IeeeSpectrumFullText/~4/VrTZobNXddc\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" /> \n</link>","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Fri, 21 Aug 2020 17:00:00 +0000","feedId":1936,"bgimg":"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3MDMyOA.jpeg","linkMd5":"0321361bb2b3ad3b4d7f6a070beb8218","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx7/cdn15@2020_6/2020/08/24/14-50-11-936_6d646f7d67ad6e59.webp","destWidth":1240,"destHeight":930,"sourceBytes":82667,"destBytes":78844,"author":"Michelle Hampson","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzI0MTAwOQ.jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx16/cdn17@2020_4/2020/08/24/14-50-13-609_964adaa282b1aac4.webp","https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3MDM1OQ.jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx23/cdn42@2020_1/2020/08/24/14-50-14-847_dea3b375d11ef596.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/IeeeSpectrumFullText/~4/VrTZobNXddc":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx5oss/cdn94@2020_2/2020/08/24/14-50-14-392_033ef4ca5d3c75f2.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598280594950},{"createdTime":"2020-08-24 22:49:54","updatedTime":"2020-08-24 22:49:54","title":"Rapid, Affordable, High Quality COVID Tests Still Scant","link":"https://spectrum.ieee.org/the-human-os/biomedical/diagnostics/rapid-affordable-high-quality-covid-tests-still-scant","description":"<link href=\"/ns/interactive/0118race-to-5g/css/5g-logo-treatments.css\" rel=\"stylesheet\"> \n <div class=\"mobileHide\"> \n  <div class=\"imgWrapper offsetLeft lt sm\"> \n   <a href=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/static/covid19-ieee-resources\"><img alt=\"IEEE COVID-19 coverage logo, link to landing page\" src=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzYxMjU3Mg.jpeg\" /></a> \n  </div> \n </div> \n <p>After nearly six months of scrutinizing the smorgasbord of COVID-19 tests available globally, an&nbsp;independent&nbsp;diagnostics evaluation group has come to some conclusions: The world greatly needs rapid, affordable tests, but the quality of such tests varies widely. By contrast, slow tests that are sent off to laboratories—the kind&nbsp;broadly used in the U.S. and Europe—are performing well.&nbsp;</p> \n <p>That’s according to the <a href=\"https://www.finddx.org/\">Foundation for Innovative New Diagnostics</a>, or FIND, headquartered in Geneva, Switzerland. <span>“People</span><span>&nbsp;in low- and middle-income countries often do not have access to laboratories,” says&nbsp;</span><a href=\"http://www.linkedin.com/in/jilian-sacks-a483a818/\"><span>Jilian Sacks</span></a><span>, FIND’s COVID-19 Evaluation Programme Lead. “So t</span><span>he ability to have&nbsp;rapid tests, especially outside the hospital and in decentralized settings, is most critical moving forward. But we are seeing that there is a lot of variability in the performance of rapid tests,</span><span>” she says.&nbsp;</span><span>&nbsp;</span></p> \n <p>Sacks’s organization in February&nbsp;took on the ambitious&nbsp;task of evaluating the performance of hundreds of COVID-19 tests&nbsp;that have been flooding the global market ever since&nbsp;the pandemic hit. <a href=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/the-human-os/biomedical/diagnostics/testing-tests-which-covid19-tests-are-most-accurate\">FIND told <em>Spectrum</em></a>&nbsp;earlier this year that it&nbsp;would subject these tests to a slew&nbsp;of coronavirus samples and make the results public on its website. This week, we checked in to see how it’s going.&nbsp;</p> \n <p>The most reliable tests, FIND has found, are molecular tests performed in a laboratory. These diagnostics&nbsp;<span>are the go-to COVID-19 testing method&nbsp;in the U.S. and Europe</span><span>. They&nbsp;</span>identify the disease by looking for the virus’s&nbsp;genetic code using a&nbsp;tool called&nbsp;<a href=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/the-human-os/biomedical/diagnostics/how-do-coronavirus-tests-work\">polymerase chain reaction, or PCR</a>.</p> \n <p>FIND has <a href=\"http://www.finddx.org/covid-19/sarscov2-eval-molecular/molecular-eval-results/\">evaluated 23 molecular&nbsp;tests</a> so far. All but two&nbsp;had a sensitivity of at least&nbsp;95%, meaning the tests&nbsp;accurately detected positive samples 95% of the time.&nbsp;“We're seeing that generally, the companies that we selected&nbsp;to evaluate have quite good performance with their assays,” so there are several good options from which ministries of health can choose,&nbsp;Sacks says.&nbsp;</p> \n <p><span>But molecular tests are slow. PCR&nbsp;</span><span>takes only a few hours to perform, but people are having to wait days to get results back&nbsp;due to the logistics of transporting samples&nbsp;to a centralized laboratory with PCR capability.&nbsp;</span></p> \n <p>“Global demand has continued to soar, and though there may be&nbsp;many companies that should be able to meet that demand, it will require continued, unprecedented scale-up of their manufacturing and distribution,” Sacks says.&nbsp;</p> \n <p>Rapid tests, on the other hand, provide results right away, on the spot, without having to send off a sample to a centralized lab. This allows people to make decisions immediately about whether or not to quarantine. And rapid tests&nbsp;are sometimes&nbsp;the only way to get tested in low-resource&nbsp;regions where access to centralized laboratories is scarce. But these tests are&nbsp;not&nbsp;as accurate as molecular tests.&nbsp;</p> \n <p>FIND is evaluating 41&nbsp;different rapid tests—5 that spot antigens and 36 that look for antibodies.&nbsp;Antigens are the parts of the virus that the immune system recognizes, and finding evidence of them means the virus is present. <span>Antibodies are produced from healthy cells as part of the body’s immune response&nbsp;to a virus, and finding those means the virus is, or was, in the body, and that the immune system responded. Both <a href=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/the-human-os/biomedical/diagnostics/how-do-coronavirus-tests-work\">types of tests</a> can be performed on-the-spot because they are less complex and don’t require a lab.</span></p> \n <p><span>FIND</span><span>&nbsp;has released&nbsp;</span><a href=\"https://www.finddx.org/covid-19/sarscov2-eval-immuno/immuno-eval-results/\" style=\"font-family: Georgia, serif; font-size: 18px;\">results</a><span>&nbsp;for four of the&nbsp;rapid antibody tests it is evaluating. Only one of them—an antibody test made by <a href=\"https://www.btnx.com/\">BTNX</a>—correctly identified&nbsp;positive cases over 90% of the time. The performance of some of</span><span>&nbsp;the other tests is so low that they provide little value. </span></p> \n <p><span>Rapid antibody tests should have a sensitivity of at least 90%, according to <a href=\"https://www.who.int/publications/m/item/covid-19-target-product-profiles-for-priority-diagnostics-to-support-response-to-the-covid-19-pandemic-v.0.1\">new draft guidelines from&nbsp;the World Health Organization (WHO)</a>&nbsp;released in early August. Rapid antigen tests should have a sensitivity of at least 70% to diagnose infection, according to the agency.</span></p> \n <p>In addition to its own program, FIND is aggregating the independent evaluations of other organizations from around the world and&nbsp;<a href=\"https://www.finddx.org/covid-19/dx-data/\">making it available online</a>.&nbsp;<span>These data&nbsp;are sorely needed. Global regulators, such as the FDA, which would normally require a lot more data from diagnostic companies before allowing a test to be used commercially, have largely stepped out of the way. This has</span><span>&nbsp;boosted innovation and hastened the availability of tests.</span><span>&nbsp;B</span><span>ut it also allows</span><span>&nbsp;inaccurate tests onto the market</span><span>, and shifts the burden of oversight to whomever wants to take on the job.&nbsp;</span><span>&nbsp;&nbsp;</span></p> \n <p><span>That’s the hole that FIND is trying to fill.&nbsp;“We have indeed heard from various ministries of health, that as companies are approaching them to offer their tests, they are looking to our website to see if we've evaluated the test and whether they feel comfortable with the results,” Sacks says.</span>​</p> \n <p>It’s good to know that someone out there is testing the tests.&nbsp;</p> \n <div class=\"mobileShow\"> \n  <h3 class=\"RptHdBackBarMobile\"><span class=\"BackArrowBlkBkgrd\">&lt;</span>&nbsp;<a href=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/static/covid19-ieee-resources\">Back to IEEE COVID-19 Resources</a></h3> \n </div> \n <img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/IeeeSpectrumFullText/~4/hPciqSe1Vxc\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" /> \n</link>","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Thu, 20 Aug 2020 18:05:00 +0000","feedId":1936,"bgimg":"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3ODI0MQ.jpeg","linkMd5":"b60fc3f3b73137da0fce050c96c11c66","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx14/cdn64@2020_3/2020/08/24/14-50-11-687_f67d1893bf58b47b.webp","destWidth":1240,"destHeight":930,"sourceBytes":142513,"destBytes":72164,"author":"Emily Waltz","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzYxMjU3Mg.jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx18/cdn17@2020_2/2020/08/24/14-50-14-803_b67305c597a3f2e7.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/IeeeSpectrumFullText/~4/hPciqSe1Vxc":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx29/cdn46@2020_1/2020/08/24/14-50-14-703_0e8127a8dd6e3e8b.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598280594951},{"createdTime":"2020-08-24 22:49:54","updatedTime":"2020-08-24 22:49:54","title":"Lucid Air EV Crushes 500 Miles-On-a-Single-Charge Barrier","link":"https://spectrum.ieee.org/cars-that-think/transportation/advanced-cars/lucid-air-ev-500-miles-on-a-single-charge","description":"<p></p> \n<p>If you’re an electric-car startup, you need an angle to make the world pay attention—especially if the media has portrayed you as a Tesla copycat for years. For <a href=\"https://lucidmotors.com/\">Lucid Motors</a>, that angle is simple: 500 miles of range—800 kilometers—on a single battery charge.</p> \n<p>That would make its upcoming Lucid Air luxury electric sedan the longest-range electric vehicle in the world, way ahead of the 402-mile rating for the <a href=\"https://www.tesla.com/blog/model-s-long-range-plus-building-first-400-mile-electric-vehicle\">2020 Tesla Model S Long Range Plus</a>, today’s range champion.</p> \n<p>The car itself will be revealed September 9, with production to begin within six months thereafter. The U.S. EPA won’t release official range estimates for the Lucid Air, however, until next spring, only a few weeks before the company says it will deliver its first production cars. So Lucid took the gamble of having its range tested by an independent third party with long experience in certifying cars to EPA test cycles.</p> \n<p>Tuesday the EV maker announced independent verification of an estimated EPA range of 517 miles, based on tests conducted by <a href=\"https://www.fev.com/en/who-we-are/locations/usa/auburn-hills.html\">FEV North America Inc.</a> in Auburn Hills, Michigan. Those tests used the EPA’s Multicycle Test procedure, codified by the SAE J1634 standard, October 2012, the same one used by every maker when tested for emissions or battery range.</p> \n<p>Your move, Tesla.</p> \n<p>Lucid unveiled its Air electric luxury sedan in April 2017 but then had to go on hiatus for two years as it sought funding to take the design into production in a purpose-built factory in Arizona. As CEO Peter Rawlinson explained <a href=\"https://www.motortrend.com/news/peter-rawlinson-lucid-motors-interview/\">to <em>Motor Trend</em> earlier this year</a>, funders at the time were excited by autonomous-vehicle technology as well as numerous other EV startups.</p> \n<p>It took two more years for Lucid to raise enough funding to move into production. It ultimately received $1.3 billion from Saudi Arabia’s sovereign-wealth fund, in exchange for more than half the company, according to <a href=\"https://www.theverge.com/2020/6/25/21302524/lucid-motors-saudi-arabia-pif-investment-majority-shareholder\">documents revealed in June</a>.</p> \n<p>The company laid off no engineers. And, in the intervening two years, the team changed the design of the Air’s battery cells, the pack voltage, the power electronics, and the electric motors that power the wheels—two motors on introduction, potentially three later on.</p> \n<p>The 2017 Air prototype used a a whopping 130-kilowatt-hour battery (against the 100 kWh of the highest-spec Model S) operating at 400 volts. It had AC induction motors and 150-kilowatt fast charging. Its range was promised to be “more than 400 miles.”</p> \n<p>The car that is to go into production next spring will have a somewhat smaller, more energy-dense pack—Lucid isn’t giving numbers at the moment—operating at around 900 volts, a pair of permanent-magnet motors, and 350-kW fast charging. The range will be at least 517 miles, per the tests, and could go higher if it can get the same 0.75 EPA “adjustment factor” for efficient air-conditioning that let the Model S Long Range Plus squeak past the 400-mile mark. (Standard factor is 0.70.)</p> \n<figure class=\"rt med-sm\" role=\"img\"> \n <img alt=\"Peter Rawlinson, CEO of Lucid Motors\" src=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3MzA3MQ.jpeg\"> \n  <figcaption>\n    Peter Rawlinson, CEO of Lucid Motors \n  </figcaption> </img> \n</figure> \n<p>The changes, as CEO Peter Rawlinson <a href=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/cars-that-think/transportation/advanced-cars/lucid-motors-says-ecar-design-all-about-efficiency\">told <em>Spectrum</em> several months ago</a>, were all in the service of efficiency—getting as much range and performance out of every kilowatt-hour by reducing air resistance, wasted energy due to resistance, and mechanical friction in every aspect of the car. To do that, he told us this week, an EV company has to engineer not only the car but every facet of its electric powertrain.</p> \n<p>The most visible aspect of an EV’s efficiency is its design. The Lucid Air offers a longer cabin and more legroom than the long-wheelbase version of the <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mercedes-Benz_S-Class\">Mercedes-Benz S-Class</a>, Rawlinson says, despite having the footprint of a mid-size car like a Honda Accord. While it “reads” big visually, he notes that it’s smaller not only than the Tesla Model S but even the Porsche Taycan, which has far less interior volume.</p> \n<p>The smaller footprint and low height gives the Air a smaller frontal area than competitors, which multiplied by the lowest drag coefficient in its class (0.21), requires less energy to displace air than other EVs. (Note that each maker does its own Cd testing, so drag coefficients from different brands may not be directly comparable.)</p> \n<p>One of Rawlinson’s passions is the necessity for any electric-car maker to engineer all the critical components of its powertrain in-house: battery pack, motors, inverters, transmission, and software. “Every detail adds up,” he stressed, returning to the theme multiple times over the course of an hour-long chat with <em>Spectrum</em>.</p> \n<p>He laid out the advantages he feels Lucid has over every other EV maker—in each area.</p> \n<h3>Battery Pack</h3> \n<p>Despite having no cars in production just yet, Lucid already has a trove of data on the performance of its batteries under extreme duty cycles. That comes from the experience its parent company <a href=\"https://www.greencarreports.com/news/atieva\">Atieva</a> garnered when it provided battery packs to all 24 cars fielded by the competing teams in the Formula E electric-car racing series.</p> \n<p>The revised pack that will launch in the Air operates at “significantly over 900 volts,” permitting fast charging at up to 350 kW&nbsp;and reducing resistance heat. Like other packs, its cells are packaged into modules (or “bricks,” as Rawlinson calls them), that make up the building blocks of the pack.</p> \n<p>Although Lucid isn’t giving details about the cell chemistry it jointly developed with battery partner <a href=\"https://www.lgchem.com/main/index\">LG Chem</a>, it uses thousands of 21700-format cylindrical cells (as Tesla does). Lucid and LG Chem together engineered a specific cell architecture with lower-resistance connectors, again in the service of reducing energy wasted as heat due to resistance.</p> \n<p>Rawlinson lauded LG Chem, as a long-term and dedicated partner. And, he added, that company’s global cell-production capabilities relieved him of that responsibility. “Thank goodness I don’t have to build a gigafactory!”</p> \n<h3>Traction Motors</h3> \n<p>One of the secrets to the Lucid’s packaging is an “ultracompact” motor, one Rawlinson says produces 55 kW per liter of volume, an output density he says is two to three times that of any other maker’s motor. One of the secrets to its efficiency is what he describes only as a novel (and heavily patented) method of cooling that puts liquid closer to the core of the motor, unlike traditional designs with coolant circulating around the periphery.</p> \n<p>The Lucid motor spins at up to 20,000 rpm, with unspecified breakthroughs in the magnetic design to reduce cogging losses—the torque produced by a permanent-magnet motor due to the interaction of the magnets and the stator slots, especially noticeable at low speeds. Again, the CEO was cagey about exactly what the design advances were. More details may emerge next month when the car is revealed.</p> \n<h3>Inverters</h3> \n<p>While Lucid’s use of <a href=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/energywise/semiconductors/devices/jayant-baligas-quest-to-make-silicon-carbide-power-electronics-succeed\">silicon-carbide MOSFETs</a> for switching doesn’t do much for the EPA test cycles that determine its range rating, Rawlinson suggests they reinforce range in the much broader array of uses in real-world driving. And he spoke sadly of a (non-Tesla) competitor’s use of a third-party inverter, noting that mixing high-voltage insulated gate bipolar transistor (IGBT) components with MOSFETs wasted energy.</p> \n<h3>Transmission&nbsp;</h3> \n<p>Rawlinson set his design team to develop the e-motors and the transmission as a “single rotational system” to ensure the most integrated and compact device possible. The gearbox hasn’t been shown yet, and the company has applied for multiple patents.</p> \n<p>One example Rawlinson was willing to discuss, however, was the 3-dimensional shape of the gear teeth themselves—an area few makers spend much time on. Lucid has patented the involute profile of the gear teeth within its transmission, optimized for performance and efficiency to transmit maximum torque under power while spinning with the least resistance otherwise.</p> \n<h3>Software</h3> \n<p>“We are as much a software company as a hardware company,” Rawlinson cheerfully admits, echoing some investors’ mantra (both pro and con) for Tesla. From the control strategies for each component of the powertrain to a user interface he promises will be “revolutionary,” the creation and optimization of the Lucid Air’s software could be the topic of a whole series of articles by itself. We left the specifics of that topic for another day.</p> \n<figure class=\"xlrg\" role=\"img\"> \n <img alt=\"DreamDrive’s array of 32 multimodal sensors includes the latest camera, radar, and ultrasonic sensors — as well as a long-distance, high-resolution LIDAR that uses laser beams to measure distance from every object in the car’s path.\" src=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3MzA4MQ.jpeg\"> \n  <figcaption class=\"hi-cap\">\n    Image: Lucid Motors \n  </figcaption> </img> \n</figure> \n<p>Before writing off Lucid as just another Tesla-wannabe, it’s worth noting CEO Rawlinson has been here before. Not only was he part of the group that created the 2002 Jaguar XJ, one of the earliest all-aluminum luxury sedans, but he led the team that engineered the actual 2012 Tesla Model S and put it into production.</p> \n<p>I first interviewed Rawlinson in January 2011, when a Model S body-in-white on display at the Detroit Auto Show was drawing a steady throng of visitors. That was the first time that other car makers began to think Tesla might actually know what it was doing.</p> \n<p>Back then Rawlinson was so exhausted he was sleeping under a conference table between interviews. But he was articulate and passionate about the challenges of building the world’s first serious long-range electric car—at a time, remember, when the then-new <a href=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/transportation/advanced-cars/nissans-allelectric-leaf-doesnt-stint-on-performance\">Nissan Leaf</a> offered an EPA rating of 73 miles.</p> \n<p>This week, Rawlinson was equally articulate, if anything more passionate, and rather better-rested. His entire mantra for the Lucid Air was to rethink every aspect of efficiency, to dispense with anxieties over battery range once and for all.</p> \n<p>“I want to move from range anxiety to range confidence,” he said. Indeed, an EPA-approved range of more than 500 miles should really end discussion over how far electric cars can travel, since it’s the rare driver whose bladder will permit that range to be used.</p> \n<p>The Lucid Air, he said, targets not the Tesla Model S—the de facto assumption by much of the press—but the Mercedes-Benz S-Class, the pinnacle of luxury sedans built and sold in high volume. In 2012, he said, few S-Class owners would have considered an electric car. Now, Tesla has proven the concept, and they’re willing to make the leap. But they won’t leave for a vehicle as stark as a Tesla, he said, so that’s where Lucid is starting.</p> \n<p>The company’s goal, he said, is to provide EVs that&nbsp;deliver enough range to end that concern for good. Then, it will launch versions with smaller, less-expensive batteries that might provide “only” 350 miles of range. And, he notes, the company’s battery packs are carefully designed for high-volume assembly, its motors engineered for mass CNC production.</p> \n<p>The Lucid CEO sounds most excited about the possibility of using the company’s ultra-efficient powertrain in smaller vehicles that would, he says, “cascade into the mass market, so the man in the street can buy an electric car” with no compromises.</p> \n<p>“That’s what drives me,” he said. The Tesla Model S? “Not a bad first effort, not bad.”</p> \n<p></p> \n<p></p> \n<p></p> \n<img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/IeeeSpectrumFullText/~4/9b7wNkKDoQc\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" />","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Tue, 11 Aug 2020 13:00:00 +0000","feedId":1936,"bgimg":"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3MzAzMg.jpeg","linkMd5":"25804602d317875d5aede29a024835e1","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx31/cdn80@2020_2/2020/08/24/14-50-11-710_ada9bb54b1ba4bc0.webp","destWidth":1240,"destHeight":930,"sourceBytes":60746,"destBytes":76018,"author":"John Voelcker","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3MzA3MQ.jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx29/cdn34@2020_1/2020/08/24/14-50-14-166_af00da377d864b3e.webp","https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3MzA4MQ.jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx12/cdn34@2020_3/2020/08/24/14-50-13-041_09ca5ae81a08def9.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/IeeeSpectrumFullText/~4/9b7wNkKDoQc":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx11/cdn34@2020_6/2020/08/24/14-50-15-276_bdbeb48e3073f7cd.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598280594950},{"createdTime":"2020-08-24 22:49:54","updatedTime":"2020-08-24 22:49:54","title":"Get More From Your Automotive Electronics Test Investment","link":"https://spectrum.ieee.org/whitepaper/get_more_from_your_automotive_electronics_test_investment","description":"<div class=\"imgWrapper ct med\"> \n <img alt=\"Keysight Automotive\" src=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY2NDY5NA.png\"> </img>\n</div> \n<p>Explore the powerful software behind Keysight’s high-precision hardware and discover how to meet emerging automotive electronics test requirements, while driving higher ROI from your existing hardware. Let Keysight help you cross the finish line ahead of your competition. Jump-start your automotive innovation today with a complimentary software trial</p>\n<img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/IeeeSpectrumFullText/~4/tCfmgU8aLA8\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" />","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Tue, 11 Aug 2020 17:30:00 +0000","feedId":1936,"bgimg":"http://feedproxy.google.com/image/MzY2NDY5NA.png","linkMd5":"7fadff43cd3c079a8fdad3c9b46cbffc","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx/cdn0@2020_1/404.jpg","sourceBytes":0,"destBytes":0,"author":"","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY2NDY5NA.png":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx4/cdn14@2020_4/2020/08/24/14-50-13-095_6f7fbaf4f5bf5ba1.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/IeeeSpectrumFullText/~4/tCfmgU8aLA8":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx4/cdn38@2020_2/2020/08/24/14-50-14-567_122c07c041d20793.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598280594950},{"createdTime":"2020-08-24 22:49:54","updatedTime":"2020-08-24 22:49:54","title":"Three Ways to Hack a Printed Circuit Board","link":"https://spectrum.ieee.org/computing/hardware/three-ways-to-hack-a-printed-circuit-board","description":"<style type=\"text/css\">.article-detail .inlay.rt, .article-detail aside.rt, .article-detail audio.rt, .article-detail canvas.rt, .article-detail div.rt, .article-detail figcaption.rt, .article-detail figure.rt, .article-detail form.rt, .article-detail iframe.rt, .article-detail img.rt, .article-detail nav.rt, .article-detail object.rt, .article-detail table.rt, .article-detail video.rt { float: right; clear: both; margin: 7px 0 3px 70px; } </style> \n<figure class=\"xlrg\" role=\"img\"> \n <img alt=\"Image of a circuit board with googly eyes on it.\" src=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3NjY3Mg.jpeg\"> \n  <div class=\"ai\"> \n   <figcaption class=\"hi-cap\">\n     Photo: Dan Saelinger \n   </figcaption> \n  </div> </img> \n</figure> \n<p><strong>In 2018,</strong> an article in Bloomberg ­Businessweek made the stupendous assertion that Chinese spy services had created back doors to servers built for <a href=\"https://www.aboutamazon.com/our-company\">Amazon</a>, <a href=\"https://www.apple.com/\">Apple</a>, and others by inserting millimeter-size chips into circuit boards.&nbsp;</p> \n<p>This claim has been roundly and specifically <a href=\"https://www.apple.com/newsroom/2018/10/what-businessweek-got-wrong-about-apple/\">refuted</a> by the companies involved and by the U.S. <a href=\"https://www.dhs.gov/news/2018/10/06/statement-dhs-press-secretary-recent-media-reports-potential-supply-chain-compromise\">Department of Homeland Security</a>. Even so, the possibility of carrying out such a stupendous hack is quite real. And there have been <a href=\"https://trust-hub.org/benchmarks/system-level-trojan\">more than a dozen documented examples of such system-level attacks</a>.</p> \n<p>We know much about <a href=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/tag/malware\">malware</a> and counterfeit ICs, but the vulnerabilities of the <a href=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/tag/PCBs\">printed circuit board</a> itself are only now starting to get the attention they deserve. We’ll take you on a tour of some of the best-known weak points in printed-circuit-board manufacturing. Fortunately, the means to shore up those points are relatively straightforward, and many of them simply amount to good engineering practice.</p> \n<p></p> \n<p><strong>In order to</strong> understand how a circuit board can be hacked, it’s worth reviewing how they are made. Printed circuit boards typically contain thousands of components. (They are also known as printed wiring boards, or PWBs, before they are populated with components.) The purpose of the PCB is, of course, to provide the structural support to hold the components in place and to provide the wiring needed to connect signals and power to the components.</p> \n<p>PCB designers start by creating two electronic documents, a schematic and a layout. The schematic describes all the components and how they are interconnected. The layout depicts the finished bare board and locates objects on the board, including both components and their labels, called <a href=\"https://dexpcb.com/manual/standard-reference-designators.htm\">reference designators</a>. (The reference designator is extremely important—most of the assembly process, and much of the design and procurement process, is tied to reference designators.)</p> \n<p>Not all of a PCB is taken up by components. Most boards include empty component footprints, called unpopulated components. This is because boards often contain extra circuitry for debugging and testing or because they are manufactured for several purposes, and therefore might have versions with more or fewer components.</p> \n<p>Once the schematic and layout have been checked, the layout is converted to a set of files. The most common file format is called “<a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gerber_format\">Gerber</a>,” or RS-274X. It consists of ASCII-formatted commands that direct shapes to appear on the board. A second ASCII-formatted file, called the drill file, shows where to place holes in the circuit board. The manufacturer then uses the files to create masks for etching, printing, and drilling the boards. Then the boards are tested.</p> \n<p>Next, “pick and place” machines put surface-mount components where they belong on the board, and the PCBs pass through an oven that melts all the solder at once. Through-hole components are placed, often by hand, and the boards pass over a machine that applies solder to all of the through-hole pins. It’s intricate work: An eight-pin, four-resistor network can cover just 2 millimeters by 1.3 mm, and some component footprints are as small as 0.25 mm by 0.13 mm. The boards are then inspected, tested, repaired as needed, and assembled further into working products.</p> \n<aside class=\"inlay xlrg\"> \n <h3 class=\"sb-hed\">Five Tempting Targets</h3> \n <h4 class=\"sb-dek\">Hacking a PCB likely means making secret additions to the many small components on a board and taking control of certain data buses</h4> \n <figure class=\"rt\" role=\"img\"> \n  <a class=\"zoom\" href=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3Njk2Mw.jpeg\" rel=\"lightbox\"><img alt=\"img\" src=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3Njk2Mw.jpeg\"><span class=\"magnifier\">&nbsp;</span></img></a> \n  <div class=\"ai\"> \n   <figcaption class=\"hi-cap\">\n     Photos: Dan Saelinger \n   </figcaption> \n  </div> \n </figure> \n <figure class=\"rt sm\" role=\"img\"> \n  <img alt=\"Close up image of a circuit board showing a threatened area: small components\" src=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3NzM0NQ.jpeg\"> \n   <div class=\"ai\"></div> </img> \n </figure> \n <p><strong>1. Small Components:</strong> Hardware hacks might need the inclusion of an extra, surreptitious component. In that case, a spot on the board with many small components is the place to hide it. Modern passive components can be mere millimeters in size and invisible to the unassisted eye. This component can be added in production (very difficult); during the “rework” step, when faulty boards are fixed (easier); or in a warehouse before or during delivery to the customer (even easier).</p> \n <hr> \n  <figure class=\"rt sm\" role=\"img\"> \n   <img alt=\"Close up image of a circuit board showing a threatened area: the power controller\" src=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3NzM0NA.jpeg\"> \n    <div class=\"ai\"></div> </img> \n  </figure> \n  <p><strong>2. Power Controller:</strong> This chip is a particularly fruitful target because it acts as the controller for all of the DC voltages that power the CPU, the graphics card, and more. It is under the control of the System Management Bus. So if a hack enables people to seize control of the SMBus, they could reset voltages in order to damage a computer or limit its operation. Control of the SMBus could also allow a hacker to interfere with communication between the CPU and onboard sensors, and that might also lead to damage.</p> \n  <hr> \n   <figure class=\"rt sm\" role=\"img\"> \n    <img alt=\"Close up image of a circuit board showing a threatened area: the low pin count bus\" src=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3NzM0Ng.jpeg\"> \n     <div class=\"ai\"></div> </img> \n   </figure> \n   <p><strong>3. Low Pin Count Bus:</strong> The connector above is attached to the LPC bus, which can link the CPU to certain legacy devices as well as to the fans and physical switches on the chassis. Perhaps just as important to hackers, the LPC bus can connect to a secure microcontroller called a Trusted Platform Module (TPM), which deals with encryption keys and various other security functions. While the TPM would very likely remain secure even if the LPC bus is compromised, a hacker could observe the traffic going to and from the TPM.</p> \n   <hr> \n    <figure class=\"rt sm\" role=\"img\"> \n     <img alt=\"Close up image of a circuit board showing a threatened area: the BIOS flash memory\" src=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3NzM1NQ.jpeg\"> \n      <div class=\"ai\"></div> </img> \n    </figure> \n    <p><strong>4. BIOS Flash Memory:</strong> The Basic Input/Output System (BIOS) flash memory holds the data needed to initialize hardware during boot up. It sits on the Serial Peripheral Interface (SPI) bus. Seizing control of the SPI bus would enable a hacker to alter hardware configurations so that a path would be open to inserting malicious code into the computer.<br> <br> <br> &nbsp;</br></br></br></p> \n    <hr> \n     <figure class=\"rt sm\" role=\"img\"> \n      <img alt=\"Close up image of a circuit board showing a threatened area: the Super I/O chip\" src=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3NzM1Ng.jpeg\"> \n       <div class=\"ai\"></div> </img> \n     </figure> \n     <p><strong>5. Super I/O Chip:</strong> The Super I/O chip controls the inputs to a variety of low-bandwidth devices, sometimes including keyboards, the mouse, certain sensors, fans, and floppy disks. The chip sits on the Low Pin Count (LPC) bus. Seizing control of the LPC bus could let hackers reduce the fan speed so that a computer will overheat. It could also let them introduce false temperature and voltage readings that could damage or shut the system down.</p> \n    </hr> \n   </hr> \n  </hr> \n </hr> \n</aside> \n<p><strong>Attacks can be</strong> made at every one of these design steps. In the first type of attack, extra components are added to the schematic. This attack is arguably the hardest to detect because the schematic is usually regarded as the most accurate reflection of the designer’s intent and thus carries the weight of authority.</p> \n<p>A variation on this theme involves adding an innocuous component to the schematic, then using a maliciously altered version of the component in production. This type of attack, in which seemingly legitimate components have <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trojan_horse_%28computing%29\">hardware Trojans</a>, is outside the scope of this article, but it should nevertheless be taken very seriously.</p> \n<p>In either case, the countermeasure is to review the schematic carefully, something that should be done in any case. One important safeguard is to run it by employees from other design groups, using their “fresh eyes” to spot an extraneous component.</p> \n<p>In a second type of attack, extra components can be added to the layout. This is a straightforward process, but because there are specific process checks to compare the layout to the schematic, it is harder to get away with it: At a minimum, a layout technician would have to falsify the results of the comparison. And combatting this form of attack is simple: Have an engineer—or, better, a group of engineers—observe the layout-to-­schematic comparison step and sign off on it.</p> \n<p>In a third type of attack, the Gerber and drill files can be altered. There are three important points on the Gerber and drill files from a security perspective: First, they’re ASCII-formatted, and therefore editable in very common text-editing tools; second, they’re human-readable; and third, they contain no built-in <a href=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/tech-talk/telecom/security/pioneers-web-cryptography-future-authentication\">cryptographic protections</a>, such as <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Digital_signature\">signatures</a> or <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Checksum\">checksums</a>. Since a complete set of ­Gerber files can be hundreds of thousands of lines, this is a very efficient mode of attack, one that is easily missed.</p> \n<p>In one example, an attacker could insert what appears to be an <a href=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/computing/hardware/antioxidants-good-for-you-good-for-your-smartphone\">electrostatic discharge</a> diode. This circuit’s design files are made up of 16 Gerber and drill files. Of the 16 files, nine would need altering; of those nine, seven would vary in a total of 79 lines, and two files need changes in about 300 lines each. The latter two files specify the power and ground planes. A more skilled attack, such as one adding vertical connections called vias, would dramatically reduce the number of lines that needed rewriting.</p> \n<p>Unprotected Gerber files are vulnerable to even a single bad actor who sneaks in at any point between the designing company and the production of the photolithographic masks. As the Gerber­ files are based on an industry standard, acquiring the knowledge to make the changes is relatively straightforward.</p> \n<p>One might argue that standard cryptographic methods of protecting files would protect Gerber files, too. While it is clear that such protections would guard a ­Gerber file in transit, it is unclear whether those protections hold when the files reach their destination. The fabrication of circuit boards almost always occurs outside the company that designs them. And, while most third-party manufacturers are reputable companies, the steps they take to protect these files are usually not documented for their customers.</p> \n<p>One way to protect files is to add a digital signature, cryptographic hash, or some other sort of authentication code to the internal contents of the file in the form of a comment. However, this protection is effective only if the mask-making process authenticates the file quite late in the process; ideally, the machines that create the photolithography masks should have the ability to authenticate a file. Alternatively, the machine could retain a cryptographic hash of the file that was actually used to create the mask, so that the manufacturer can audit the process. In either case, the mask-making machine would itself require secure handling.</p> \n<p></p> \n<aside class=\"inlay pullquote rt med\">\n  Knowing how to sabotage a PC is only half the job. Attackers also have to know what the best targets are on a computer motherboard.&nbsp;&nbsp; &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; \n</aside> \n<p><strong>If bad actors succeed </strong>at one of these three attacks, they can add an actual, physical component to the assembled circuit board. This can occur in three ways.</p> \n<p>First, the extra component can be added in production. This is difficult because it requires altering the supply chain to add the component to the procurement process, programming the pick-and-place machine to place the part, and attaching a reel of parts to the machine. In other words, it would require the cooperation of several bad actors, a conspiracy that might indicate the work of a corporation or a state.</p> \n<p>Second, the extra component can be added in the repair-and-rework area—a&nbsp;much easier target than production. It’s common for assembled circuit boards to require reworking by hand. For example, on a board with 2,000 components, the first-pass yield—the fraction of boards with zero defects—might be below 70&nbsp;percent. The defective boards go to a technician who then adds or removes components by hand; a single technician could easily add dozens of surreptitious components per day. While not every board would have the extra component, the attack might still succeed, especially if there was a collaborator in the shipping area to ship the hacked boards to targeted customers. Note that succeeding at this attack (altered Gerber files, part inserted in repair, unit selectively shipped) requires only three people.</p> \n<p>Third, a component can be added by hand to a board after production—in a warehouse, for instance. The fact that an in-transit attack is possible may require companies to inspect incoming boards to confirm that unpopulated parts remain unpopulated.</p> \n<p></p> \n<p><strong><span>Kn</span>owing how to</strong> sabotage a PCB is only half the job. Attackers also have to know what the best targets are on a computer motherboard. They’ll try the data buses, specifically those with two things in common—low data rates and low pin counts. High-speed buses, such as SATA, M.2, and DDR are so sensitive to data rates that the delay of an extra component would very likely keep them from working correctly. And a component with a smaller number of pins is simpler to sneak into a design; therefore, buses with low pin counts are easier targets. On a PC motherboard, there are three such buses.</p> \n<p>The first is the System Management Bus (SMBus), which controls the voltage regulators and clock frequency on most PC motherboards. It’s based on the two-wire <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/I%C2%B2C\">Inter-IC (I2C)</a> standard created by Philips Semiconductor back in 1982. That standard has no encryption, and it allows a number of connected devices to directly access critical onboard components, such as the power supply, independently of the CPU.</p> \n<p>A surreptitious component on an SMBus could enable two types of attacks against a system. It could change the voltage settings of a regulator and damage components. It could also interfere with communications between the processor and onboard sensors, either by impersonating another device or by intentionally interfering with incoming data.</p> \n<p>The second target is the Serial Peripheral Interface (SPI) bus, a four-wire bus created by Motorola in the mid-1980s. It’s used by most modern flash-memory parts, and so is likely to be the bus on which the important code, such as the BIOS (Basic Input/Output System), is accessed.</p> \n<figure class=\"lt med\" role=\"img\"> \n <img alt=\"Image of a circuit board with googly eyes on it.\" src=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3NjczMg.gif\"> \n  <div class=\"ai\"> \n   <figcaption class=\"hi-cap\">\n     Gif: Dan Saelinger \n   </figcaption> \n  </div> </img> \n</figure> \n<p>A well-considered attack against the SPI bus could alter any portion of the data that is read from an attached memory chip. Modifications to the BIOS as it is being accessed could change hardware configurations done during the boot process, leaving a path open for malicious code.</p> \n<p>The third target is the LPC (Low Pin Count) bus, and it’s particularly attractive because an attack can compromise the operation of the computer, provide remote access to power and other vital control functions, and compromise the security of the boot process. This bus carries seven mandatory signals and up to six optional signals; it is used to connect a computer’s CPU to legacy devices, such as serial and parallel ports, or to physical switches on the chassis, and in many modern PCs, its signals control the fans.</p> \n<p>The LPC bus is such a vulnerable point because many servers use it to connect a separate management processor to the system. This processor, called the baseboard management controller (BMC), can perform basic housekeeping functions even if the main processor has crashed or the operating system has not been installed. It’s convenient because it permits remote control, repair, and diagnostics of server components. Most BMCs have a dedicated Ethernet port, and so an attack on a BMC can also result in network access.</p> \n<p>The BMC also has a pass-through connection to the SPI bus, and many processors load their BIOS through that channel. This is a purposeful design decision, as it permits the BIOS to be patched remotely via the BMC.</p> \n<p>Many motherboards also use the LPC bus to access hardware implementing the Trusted Platform Module (TPM) standard, which provides cryptographic keys and a range of other services to secure a computer and its software.</p> \n<p></p> \n<p><strong>Start your search</strong> for surreptitious components with these buses. You can search them by machine: At the far end of the automation is a <a href=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/riskfactor/computing/hardware/this-tech-would-have-spotted-the-secret-chinese-chip-in-seconds\">system developed</a> by <a href=\"http://tehranipoor.ece.ufl.edu/\">Mark M. Tehranipoor</a>, director of the Florida Institute for Cybersecurity Research, in Gainesville. It uses optical scans, microscopy, X-ray tomography, and artificial intelligence to compare a PCB and its components with the intended design. Or you can do the search by hand, which consists of four rounds of checks. While these manual methods may take time, they don’t need to be done on every single board, and they require little technical skill.</p> \n<p>In the first round, check the board for components that lack a reference designator. This is a bright red flag; there is no way that a board so hobbled could be manufactured in a normal production process. Finding such a component is a strong indication of an attack on the board layout files (that is, the Gerber and drill files), because that step is the likeliest place to add a component without adding a reference designator. Of course, a component without a reference designator is a major design mistake and worth catching under any circumstances.</p> \n<p>In the second round of checks, make sure that every reference designator is found in the schematic, layout, and bill of materials. A bogus reference designator is another clear indication that someone has tampered with the board layout files.</p> \n<p>In the third round, focus on the shape and size of the component footprints. For example, if a four-pin part is on the schematic and the layout or board has an eight-pin footprint, this is clear evidence of a hack.</p> \n<p>The fourth round of checks should examine all the unpopulated parts of the board. Although placing components in an unpopulated spot may well be the result of a genuine mistake, it may also be a sign of sabotage, and so it needs to be checked for both reasons.</p> \n<p>As you can see, modern motherboards, with their thousands of sometimes mote-size components, are quite vulnerable to subversion. Some of those exploits make it possible to gain access to vital system functions. Straightforward methods can detect and perhaps deter most of these attacks. As with malware, heightened sensitivity to the issue and well-planned scrutiny can make attacks unlikely and unsuccessful.</p> \n<h2>About the Author</h2> \n<p><a href=\"https://www.southalabama.edu/colleges/engineering/ece/russ.html\">Samuel H. Russ</a> is an associate professor of electrical engineering at the University of South Alabama. <a href=\"https://www.linkedin.com/in/jacob-gatlin-a67ba4126\">Jacob Gatlin</a> is a Ph.D. candidate at the university.</p> \n<img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/IeeeSpectrumFullText/~4/brMZn8TqrdE\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" />","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Fri, 21 Aug 2020 15:00:00 +0000","feedId":1936,"bgimg":"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3NjY3Mw.jpeg","linkMd5":"ef3150136debe1506f4b25d0334984f9","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx8/cdn23@2020_6/2020/08/24/14-50-11-661_e547f11111d1ee01.webp","destWidth":1240,"destHeight":930,"sourceBytes":114590,"destBytes":68492,"author":"Samuel H. Russ","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3NjY3Mg.jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn65@2020_5/2020/08/24/14-50-15-064_f97b57954ad93978.webp","https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3Njk2Mw.jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx10/cdn58@2020_6/2020/08/24/14-50-13-249_a1aed45868889175.webp","https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3NzM0NQ.jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx32/cdn90@2020_4/2020/08/24/14-50-14-448_6b33759c7be44778.webp","https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3NzM0NA.jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx11/cdn22@2020_2/2020/08/24/14-50-13-691_30a35eaca3ebf628.webp","https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3NzM0Ng.jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx24/cdn22@2020_3/2020/08/24/14-50-14-476_6eac75c7f1c2f003.webp","https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3NzM1NQ.jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx27/cdn55@2020_2/2020/08/24/14-50-11-817_b97621fb347e399f.webp","https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3NzM1Ng.jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx7/cdn29@2020_4/2020/08/24/14-50-13-243_5135e2f30a5578a0.webp","https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3NjczMg.gif":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx22/cdn54@2020_5/2020/08/24/14-50-13-226_312860568b6ecc07.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/IeeeSpectrumFullText/~4/brMZn8TqrdE":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx31/cdn94@2020_4/2020/08/24/14-50-12-712_8b2f76333189d2e3.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598280594947},{"createdTime":"2020-08-24 22:49:54","updatedTime":"2020-08-24 22:49:54","title":"Engineers in Rio de Janeiro Roll Up Their Sleeves to Repair Ventilators","link":"https://spectrum.ieee.org/news-from-around-ieee/the-institute/ieee-member-news/engineers-in-rio-de-janeiro-roll-up-their-sleeves-to-repair-ventilators","description":"<link href=\"/ns/interactive/0118race-to-5g/css/5g-logo-treatments.css\" rel=\"stylesheet\"> \n <style type=\"text/css\">.entry-content .tisubhead { color: #999999; font-family: verdana; font-size: 14px; font-weight: bold; letter-spacing: 1px; margin-bottom: -5px !important; text-transform: uppercase; } .tiblogopener { color: #a17e54; font-family: Theinhardt-Medium, sans-serif; letter-spacing: 1px; margin-right: 10px; font-weight: bold; text-transform: uppercase; } </style> \n <div class=\"mobileHide\"> \n  <div class=\"imgWrapper offsetLeft lt sm\"> \n   <a href=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/static/covid19-ieee-resources\"><img alt=\"IEEE COVID-19 coverage logo, link to landing page\" src=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzYxMjU3Mg.jpeg\" /></a> \n  </div> \n </div> \n <p><span class=\"tiblogopener\">THE INSTITUTE </span>Volunteers from the <a href=\"http://www.ieee.org.br/rio-de-janeiro/a-secao/\"><span class=\"ListLabel1\">IEEE Rio de Janeiro Section</span></a> and others are fighting the COVID-19 outbreak by repairing broken mechanical ventilators used in public hospitals. Ventilators are commonly used to treat COVID-19 patients with severe respiratory distress. There are simply not enough professionals available to repair the equipment in the short time frame due to the large demand for machines, says Yuri Bastos Gabrich, one of the IEEE members working on the Engineers Assisting Ventilator Maintenance project.&nbsp;</p> \n <p>“The pandemic has increased the demand for new ventilators worldwide, which has also complicated the [ability to] purchase new ones,” he says.</p> \n <p></p> \n <p>To help cover the expenses of their project, the IEEE volunteers applied to the&nbsp;<a href=\"https://hac.ieee.org/funding-opportunities/covid-19-projects/\"><span class=\"ListLabel3\">COVID-19 Fund</span></a> sponsored by the&nbsp;<a href=\"https://hac.ieee.org/\"><span class=\"ListLabel3\">IEEE Humanitarian Activities Committee</span></a>&nbsp;and the&nbsp;<span class=\"ListLabel3\"><a href=\"https://sight.ieee.org/\">IEEE Special Interest Group on Humanitarian Technology</a>. The project was&nbsp;</span>awarded <a href=\"https://cmte.ieee.org/r9-hac/covid-19-projects/\"><span class=\"ListLabel1\">US $5,000</span></a>. Out of the 30 volunteers working on the project, 13 are IEEE members. In addition to Gabrich, they include Senior Member Oumar Diene and Member Gidy Carolina Florez Navarro.</p> \n <p>In addition, the project has the support of the <a href=\"https://www.saude.rj.gov.br/\"><span class=\"ListLabel5\">Rio de Janeiro State Health Department</span></a>.</p> \n <p>Also assisting the hospitals is the <a href=\"http://www.cefet-rj.br/\"><span class=\"ListLabel2\">Federal Center for Technological Education of Rio de Janeiro</span></a> (CEFET/RJ), which launched the “Núcleo de Reparo de Ventiladores Mecânicos” (Repair Center of Mechanical Ventilators) with several partner institutions. Repairs are done at a maintenance hub located at CEFET/RJ Maracanã campus. Because all the onsite educational activities were suspended due to the pandemic, minor renovations were made to lab classrooms to turn them into repair rooms, Gabrich says. Only the volunteers doing the repairs have access to the facilities.</p> \n <p>“Individuals and several industry partners are [assisting with] the initiative, and creating a multidisciplinary team with a range of knowledge and tools that together impact the society for the good,” Gabrich says. “In addition to helping to save lives, the IEEE members and nonmembers are getting the opportunity to learn about topics such as the treatments being used for COVID-19 patients, equipment maintenance, test management, equipment care and safety, and health monitoring.”</p> \n <p>Since June, the volunteers have repaired eight ventilators, and five more machines are in the process of being fixed and will be ready for use later this month.</p> \n <p><em><a href=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/the-institute\"><span class=\"ListLabel4\">The Institute</span></a></em>&nbsp;asked Gabrich about the project.</p> \n <p><em>This interview has been edited and condensed for clarity.</em></p> \n <p></p> \n <figure class=\"xlrg\" role=\"img\"> \n  <img alt=\"Heitor Mendes, Yuri Gabrich, Felipe Piancó, Julia Neri, Gidy Florez, Brenno Tavares, Neilson Dantas, and Marcela Brandão.\" src=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3ODEwNQ.jpeg\"> \n   <figcaption class=\"hi-cap\">\n     Photo:&nbsp;Gidy Florez \n   </figcaption> \n   <figcaption>\n     Other volunteers working to repair the ventilators are [from left] Heitor Mendes, Yuri Gabrich, Felipe Piancó, Julia Neri, Gidy Florez, Brenno Tavares, Neilson Dantas, and Marcela Brandão are ready to get to work. \n   </figcaption> </img> \n </figure> \n <p><strong>What problem are you trying to solve?</strong></p> \n <p>The lack of mechanical ventilators to meet the huge need for treatment of COVID-19 patients in public hospitals in Rio de Janeiro State.</p> \n <p><strong>What technologies are you using?</strong></p> \n <p>To gather information about the ventilators that need to be repaired, a multidisciplinary team of professors and researchers from the CEFET/RJ Nova Iguaçu campus developed the Android app, <a href=\"https://play.google.com/store/apps/details?id=com.wwmm.sostecsaude&amp;hl=en\"><span class=\"InternetLink\">SOSTecSaúde</span></a>. This app connects hospitals and medical centers asking for help with those who can [provide] aid. This is the entryway to start the repair activities at the maintenance hub.</p> \n <p>For the security of equipment, several cameras with facial recognition were installed throughout the maintenance hub to track all steps the ventilators pass through—from the arrival and departure gate to each repair room. This visual monitoring is offered by the initiative’s partner <a href=\"https://www.gsol.com.br/\"><span class=\"InternetLink\">GSOL Energy</span></a>, [an IT company] that has provided network infrastructure and apparatus to monitor the hub, 24/7.</p> \n <p>For monitoring the health of on-site volunteers, we are using a predictive data analysis of their heart rate and actigraphic data. [Actigraphy is a non-invasive method of monitoring a person’s cycle of rest and activity]. This technology is offered by the initiative’s partner <a href=\"http://ezrest.com.br/\"><span class=\"ListLabel5\">EzRest</span></a> , which works with professional fatigue management. Volunteers wear the <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Xiaomi-Waterproof-Smartband-Touchpad-Wristbands/dp/B07DG3P996\"><span class=\"ListLabel5\">Xiaomi Mi Band 3</span></a> [a fitness tracker] to collect the data, which is sent to the EzRest AI system that monitors their health condition. When a different pattern [of readings] is found, the system indicates a health risk but it does not identify the risk or its cause. However, when a moderate to high health risk is identified, [it is recommended that] the volunteer take a break from work and seek medical attention.</p> \n <p><strong>What training is provided to those who fix the machine?</strong></p> \n <p>Volunteers received eight hours of training divided over two days. The theoretical lessons formed the basis for the almost 58 hours of hands-on activities that came in the following days.&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</p> \n <figure class=\"xlrg\" role=\"img\"> \n  <img alt=\"Disassembling and cleaning the internal part of the ventilator.\" src=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3NDU1MA.jpeg\"> \n   <figcaption class=\"hi-cap\">\n     Photo: Yuri Gabrich \n   </figcaption> \n   <figcaption>\n     Disassembling and&nbsp;cleaning the internal part of the ventilator are [from left] Marcela Brandão, Julio César Santos, and Felipe Piancó. \n   </figcaption> </img> \n </figure> \n <p>Wanderson Alves de Araújo gave volunteers instructions about how to handle and repair the mechanical ventilators using the brands Maquet and Servo-S [Servo-based technology]. De Araújo is the technical supervisor for Nova Service, one of [the initiative’s] partners. Beyond the tips and tricks he taught us, he also visits the hub once a week to calibrate ventilators before sending them back to hospitals.</p> \n <p>Cristiane Duarte Barbosa, a nurse working on the front lines, taught [us] about how to adjust ventilator parameters. We use the GE iVent-20 model. Cristiane and Julio César Santos are both professors of nursing at the CEFET/RJ Nova Iguaçu campus. They explained several [interesting things] about coronavirus treatment.</p> \n <p>Alexandre Pinhel, an IEEE senior member, and [an] engineer at <a href=\"https://www.furnas.com.br/\"><span class=\"ListLabel5\">Eletrobras Furnas</span></a>, [a power utility company] explained electrostatic discharge (ESD). He taught us how to properly manipulate electronic boards without damaging them or unconsciously compromising their lifetime. He also gave each volunteer a copy of his book “Basic Concepts for Protection Against Static Electricity: The Problem of Electrostatic Discharges in Electronic Components.”</p> \n <p>For the rest of the project’s schedule, the trained volunteers are responsible for passing on the acquired knowledge to new volunteers that may join the team.&nbsp;Volunteers always work under the supervision of specialized engineers.</p> \n <p><strong>What medical facilities and other organizations are you helping?</strong></p> \n <p>We are repairing ventilators from Raul Sertã Municipal Hospital, Getúlio Vargas State Hospital, Eduardo Rabelo State Hospital, and Alberto Torres State Hospital.</p> \n <figure class=\"xlrg\" role=\"img\"> \n  <img alt=\"ulia Neri, Neilson Dantas, Felipe Piancó, Alexandre Pinhel, Yuri Gabrich, and Wanderson Araújo\" src=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3ODEwNg.jpeg\"> \n   <figcaption class=\"hi-cap\">\n     Photo:&nbsp;Alexandre Pinhel \n   </figcaption> \n   <figcaption>\n     On their snack time break in the repair hub are [from left] Julia Neri, Neilson Dantas, Felipe Piancó, Alexandre Pinhel, Yuri Gabrich, and Wanderson Araújo. \n   </figcaption> </img> \n </figure> \n <p><strong>What challenges have you faced, and how did you overcome them?</strong></p> \n <p>In the beginning, the urgency to find partners, learn new subjects in [a short time], and organize all the activities to receive equipment and [train] volunteers became an unhealthy and stressful job for CEFET’s committee. They were spending hours working without stop, and even without drinking water. The end of the day was always bad.</p> \n <p>The solution was to implement a snack time. It brought happy smiles back (even with a facial mask), and has transformed the team’s stop time to self-care and recharge time. The snack time happens in the open space of the hub, with each volunteer sitting apart from each other, maintaining social distancing.</p> \n <p>Another challenge was to deal with a large amount of information and activities. Volunteers, from seniors to beginners, came to understand the need for better communication about what was going on and decided to divide responsibilities to get better results. Therefore, while a couple of them give attention to the process documentation, some focus on repairing equipment, while others contact partners and do some marketing.</p> \n <p>For instance, the <a href=\"https://yp.ieee.org/tag/ieee-young-professionals-rio-de-janeiro/\"><span class=\"ListLabel5\">IEEE Young Professionals</span></a> Rio de Janeiro Section leaders, Member Marcos Magalhães and Member Fernando Lima, are remotely contributing to the project’s social media and holding weekly meetings to align planning and execution of the activities.</p> \n <p>The project’s treasurer (as well as the IEEE section’s), Thomas Moreira Campello, an IEEE graduate student member who is also working remotely, is responsible for paying for the snacks. José Andrés Santisteban Larrea, an IEEE senior member and chair of IEEE <a href=\"https://ias.ieee.org/\"><span class=\"ListLabel5\">Industrial Applications</span></a>/<a href=\"http://www.ieee-ies.org/\"><span class=\"ListLabel5\">Industrial Electronics</span></a>, and <a href=\"https://www.ieee-pels.org/\"><span class=\"ListLabel5\">Power Electronics</span></a> joint chapter, is organizing live events to spread the [word about the] initiative and gather funds, even while working from home.</p> \n <p>The organizers of the initiative, Alexandre Pinhel and Mauricio Motta have reached [out to] several industry representatives and received the required support to set up the hub.</p> \n <p>The wish to impact society for the good is the gear that moves this group of dedicated volunteers towards the efforts to save lives.</p> \n <div class=\"mobileShow\"> \n  <h3 class=\"RptHdBackBarMobile\"><span class=\"BackArrowBlkBkgrd\">&lt;</span>&nbsp;<a href=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/static/covid19-ieee-resources\">Back to IEEE COVID-19 Resources</a></h3> \n </div> \n <img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/IeeeSpectrumFullText/~4/nkxMF6s0yM8\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" /> \n</link>","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Mon, 17 Aug 2020 18:00:00 +0000","feedId":1936,"bgimg":"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3NDUxNQ.jpeg","linkMd5":"0f657b1612af618a5d11d09a8b3b9b79","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx22/cdn28@2020_5/2020/08/24/14-50-11-841_8f582dabd78ef6c4.webp","destWidth":1240,"destHeight":930,"sourceBytes":232537,"destBytes":130352,"author":"The Institute’s Editorial Staff","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzYxMjU3Mg.jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx18/cdn17@2020_2/2020/08/24/14-50-14-803_b67305c597a3f2e7.webp","https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3ODEwNQ.jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx2/cdn36@2020_3/2020/08/24/14-50-11-857_ff695393d5001460.webp","https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3NDU1MA.jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx6/cdn98@2020_3/2020/08/24/14-50-13-275_bf6fc8077d121986.webp","https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3ODEwNg.jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx18/cdn5@2020_3/2020/08/24/14-50-13-575_b30f7505c466e1b9.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/IeeeSpectrumFullText/~4/nkxMF6s0yM8":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx33/cdn25@2020_3/2020/08/24/14-50-14-510_5df53f91b7966340.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598280594951},{"createdTime":"2020-08-24 22:49:54","updatedTime":"2020-08-24 22:49:54","title":"The CPU’s Silent Partner: The Coprocessor’s Role Is Often Unappreciated","link":"https://spectrum.ieee.org/computing/hardware/the-cpus-silent-partner-the-coprocessors-role-is-often-unappreciated","description":"<figure class=\"rt med\" role=\"img\"> \n <img alt=\"Illustration of a person walking up stairs made of smart phones.  \" src=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3ODE4OA.jpeg\"> \n  <div class=\"ai\"> \n   <figcaption class=\"hi-cap\">\n     Illustration: Dan Page \n   </figcaption> \n  </div> </img>\n</figure> \n<p><strong><span>One reason</span></strong> the PC has endured for nearly 40 years is that its design was almost entirely open: No patents restricted reproduction of the fully documented hardware and firmware. When you bought a PC, IBM gave you everything you needed to manufacture your own <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IBM_PC_compatible\">clone</a>. That openness seeded an explosion of PC compatibles, the foundation of the computing environment we enjoy today.</p> \n<p>In one corner of the original PC’s motherboard, alongside the underpowered-but-epochal 8088 CPU, sat an empty socket. It awaited an upgrade it rarely received: an <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intel_8087\">8087 floating-point coprocessor</a>.</p> \n<p>Among the most complex chips of its day, the 8087 accelerated mathematical computations—in particular, the calculation of transcendental functions—by two orders of magnitude. While not something you’d need for a <a href=\"https://aresluna.org/attached/computerhistory/articles/spreadsheets/lotus123review\">Lotus 1-2-3</a> spreadsheet, for early users of <a href=\"https://www.autodesk.com/products/autocad/overview\">AutoCAD</a> those functions were absolutely essential. Pop that chip into your PC and rendering detailed computer-aided-design (CAD) drawings no longer felt excruciatingly slow. That speed boost didn’t come cheap, though. One vendor sold the upgrade for US $295—almost $800 in today’s dollars.</p> \n<p>Recently, I purchased a PC whose CPU runs a million times as fast as that venerable 8088 and uses a million times as much RAM. That computer cost me about as much as an original PC—but in 2020 dollars, it’s worth only a third as much. Yet the proportion of my spend that went into a top-of-the-line graphics processing unit (GPU) was the same as what I would have invested in an 8087 back in the day.</p> \n<p>Although I rarely use CAD, I do write math-intensive code for virtual or augmented reality and videogrammetry. My new coprocessor—a hefty slice of <a href=\"https://www.nvidia.com/en-us/\">Nvidia</a> silicon—performs its computations at 200 million times the speed of its ancestor.</p> \n<p>That kind of performance bump can only partially be attributed to Moore’s Law. Half or more of the speedup derives from the massive parallelism designed into modern GPUs, which are capable of simultaneously executing several thousand pixel-shader programs (to compute position, color, and other attributes when rendering objects).</p> \n<p>Such massive parallelism has its direct analogue in another, simultaneous revolution: the advent of pervasive connectivity. Since the late 1990s, it’s been a mistake to conceive of a PC as a stand-alone device. Through the Web, each PC has been plugged into a coprocessor of a different sort: the millions of other PCs that are similarly connected.</p> \n<p>The computing hardware we quickly grew to depend on was eventually refined into a smartphone, representing the essential parts of a PC, trimmed to accommodate a modest size and power budget. And smartphones are even better networked than early PCs were. So we shouldn’t think of the coprocessor in a smartphone as its GPU, which helps draw pretty pictures on the screen. The real coprocessor is the connected capacity of some 4 billion other smartphone-­carrying people, each capable of sharing with and learning from one another through the Web or on various social-media platforms. It’s something that brings out both the best and worst in us.</p> \n<p>We all now have powerful tools at our fingertips for connecting with others. When we plug ourselves into this global coprocessor, we can put our heads together to imagine, plan, and produce—or to conspire, harass, and thwart. We can search and destroy, or we can create and share. With the great power that this technology confers comes great responsibility. That’s something we should remind ourselves of every time we peer at our screens.</p> \n<p><em>This article appears in the September 2020 print issue as “The CPU’s Silent Partner.”</em></p>\n<img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/IeeeSpectrumFullText/~4/HJJ-z_ZP39I\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" />","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Thu, 20 Aug 2020 19:30:00 +0000","feedId":1936,"bgimg":"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3ODE4OQ.jpeg","linkMd5":"0eb111c9331487c6fa6bdf4fc73e57dc","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx19/cdn48@2020_4/2020/08/24/14-50-12-042_133a9a37ef3fb9d9.webp","destWidth":600,"destHeight":586,"sourceBytes":296351,"destBytes":19316,"author":"Mark Pesce","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3ODE4OA.jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx15/cdn71@2020_4/2020/08/24/14-50-11-752_6e1b5e38e646a89b.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/IeeeSpectrumFullText/~4/HJJ-z_ZP39I":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx30/cdn81@2020_4/2020/08/24/14-50-12-024_e7eafc08ebe2a7ae.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598280594949},{"createdTime":"2020-08-24 22:49:54","updatedTime":"2020-08-24 22:49:54","title":"CaseCrawler Adds Tiny Robotic Legs to Your Phone","link":"https://spectrum.ieee.org/automaton/robotics/robotics-hardware/casecrawler-adds-tiny-robotic-legs-to-your-phone","description":"<link href=\"/ns/interactive/0118race-to-5g/css/5g-logo-treatments.css\" rel=\"stylesheet\"> \n <div class=\"mobileHide\"> \n  <div class=\"imgWrapper offsetLeft lt sm\"> \n   <a href=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/static/journal-watch\"><img alt=\"Journal Watch report logo, link to report landing page\" src=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzI0MTAwOQ.jpeg\" /></a> \n  </div> \n </div> \n <p>Most of us have a fairly rational expectation that if we put our cellphone down somewhere, it will stay in that place until we pick it up again. Normally, this is exactly what you’d want, but there are exceptions, like when you put your phone down in not quite the right spot on a wireless charging pad without noticing, or when you’re lying on the couch and your phone is <em>juuust</em> out of reach no matter how much you stretch.</p> \n <p>Roboticists from the <a href=\"https://www.biorobotics.snu.ac.kr/\">Biorobotics Laboratory at Seoul National University</a> in South Korea have solved both of these problems, and many more besides, by developing a cellphone case with little robotic legs, endowing your phone with the ability to skitter around autonomously. And unlike most of the phone-robot hybrids we’ve seen in the past, this one actually does look like a legit case for your phone.</p> \n <!--nextpage--> \n <p><iframe allowfullscreen=\"\" frameborder=\"0\" height=\"349\" src=\"//www.youtube.com/embed/lzEUn2CAuFs?rel=0\" width=\"620\"></iframe></p> \n <p>CaseCrawler is much chunkier than a form-fitting case, but it’s not offensively bigger than one of those chunky battery cases. It’s only 24 millimeters thick (excluding the motor housing), and the total weight is just under 82 grams. Keep in mind that this case is in fact an entire robot, and also not at all optimized for being an actual phone case, so it’s easy to imagine how it could get a lot more svelte—for example, it currently includes a small battery that would be unnecessary if it instead tapped into the phone for power.</p> \n <p>The technology inside is pretty amazing, since it involves legs that can retract all the way flat while also supporting a significant amount of weight. The legs work sort of like your legs do, in that there’s a knee joint that can only bend one way. To move the robot forward, a linkage (attached to a motor through a gearbox) pushes the leg back against the ground, as the knee joint keeps the leg straight. On the return stroke, the joint allows the leg to fold, making it compliant so that it doesn’t exert force on the ground. The transmission that sends power from the gearbox to the legs is just 1.5-millimeter thick, but this incredibly thin and lightweight mechanical structure is quite powerful. A non-phone case version of the robot, weighing about 23 g, is able to crawl at 21 centimeters per second while carrying a payload of just over 300 g. That’s more than 13 times its body weight.</p> \n <p>The researchers plan on exploring how robots like these could make other objects movable that would otherwise not be.
