They’d also like to add some autonomy, which (at least for the phone case version) could be as straightforward as leveraging the existing sensors on the phone. And as to when you might be able to buy one of these—we’ll keep you updated, but the good news is that it seems to be fundamentally inexpensive enough that it may actually crawl out of the lab one day.</p> \n <h4>“CaseCrawler: A Lightweight and Low-Profile Crawling Phone Case Robot,” by Jongeun Lee, Gwang-Pil Jung, Sang-Min Baek, Soo-Hwan Chae, Sojung Yim, Woongbae Kim, and Kyu-Jin Cho from Seoul National University, appears in the October issue of <em>IEEE Robotics and Automation Letters</em>.</h4> \n <div class=\"mobileShow\"> \n  <h3 class=\"RptHdBackBarMobile\"><span class=\"BackArrowBlkBkgrd\">&lt;</span>&nbsp;<a href=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/static/journal-watch\">Back to IEEE Journal Watch</a></h3> \n </div> \n <img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/IeeeSpectrumFullText/~4/uzUur1KQ_uU\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" /> \n</link>","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Tue, 11 Aug 2020 20:52:00 +0000","feedId":1936,"bgimg":"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3MzYzNg.jpeg","linkMd5":"1bb438633631f1192ea2ebc8ad35f776","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx10/cdn30@2020_4/2020/08/24/14-50-12-743_e86246fc586ab15f.webp","destWidth":1240,"destHeight":833,"sourceBytes":189185,"destBytes":27146,"author":"Evan Ackerman","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzI0MTAwOQ.jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx16/cdn17@2020_4/2020/08/24/14-50-13-609_964adaa282b1aac4.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/IeeeSpectrumFullText/~4/uzUur1KQ_uU":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx31/cdn2@2020_1/2020/08/24/14-50-13-437_37cb338a6a15f7b2.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598280594950},{"createdTime":"2020-08-24 22:49:54","updatedTime":"2020-08-24 22:49:54","title":"Startup’s Thermal Imaging and AR System for Firefighters Joins the COVID-19 Fight","link":"https://spectrum.ieee.org/the-institute/ieee-member-news/startups-thermal-imaging-and-ar-system-for-firefighters-joins-the-covid19-fight","description":"<link href=\"/ns/interactive/0118race-to-5g/css/5g-logo-treatments.css\" rel=\"stylesheet\"> \n <style type=\"text/css\">.entry-content .tisubhead { color: #999999; font-family: verdana; font-size: 14px; font-weight: bold; letter-spacing: 1px; margin-bottom: -5px !important; text-transform: uppercase; } .tiopener { color: #0f4994; font-family: Theinhardt-Medium, sans-serif; letter-spacing: 1px; margin-right: 10px; font-weight: bold; text-transform: uppercase; } </style> \n <figure class=\"xlrg\" role=\"img\"> \n  <img alt=\"Photograph of a firefighter with the Longan Vision FVS augmented-reality visor showing thermal imaging\" src=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3MzM1OA.jpeg\"> \n   <div class=\"ai\"> \n    <figcaption class=\"hi-cap\">\n      Photo-illustraiton: Longan Vision \n    </figcaption> \n    <figcaption>\n      An illustration of a firefighter wearing Longan Vision’s&nbsp;AR visor with thermal imaging cameras over his helmet. The Fusion Vision System allows the first responder to see through smoke, locate victims, and find the source of the fire. \n    </figcaption> \n   </div> </img> \n </figure> \n <div class=\"mobileHide\"> \n  <div class=\"imgWrapper offsetLeft lt sm\"> \n   <a href=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/static/covid19-ieee-resources\"><img alt=\"IEEE COVID-19 coverage logo, link to landing page\" src=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzYxMjU3Mg.jpeg\" /></a> \n  </div> \n </div> \n <p><span class=\"tiopener\">THE INSTITUTE </span>Before the coronavirus pandemic hit Canada, <a href=\"https://www.linkedin.com/in/enzo-jia-86454569/\">Enzo Jia</a> was busy developing the Fusion Vision System, an augmented-reality (AR) visor with thermal imaging to help firefighters see through smoke.</p> \n <p>“I always wanted to design something to help humans enhance their vision and also to see something they cannot with the naked eye,” Jia told <em>The Institute</em> in March. He is chief executive of <a href=\"https://www.longanvision.com/\">Longan Vision</a>, a startup he helped found. “I really want to help firefighters and first responders enhance their vision by using AR technologies.”</p> \n <p>The startup, based in Hamilton, Ont., was named a 2020 <a href=\"https://entrepreneurship.ieee.org/entrepreneurship-stars/\">IEEE Entrepreneurship Star</a> at this year’s <a href=\"https://www.cnet.com/ces/\">Consumer Electronics Show</a>, held in January. The program recognizes early-stage companies that have the potential of bringing to market engineering-driven innovations in IEEE’s field of interest. Along with the recognition, awardees get a free year of IEEE membership.</p> \n <p>In the months before the pandemic, the company had developed a prototype of its Fusion Vision System and had demonstrated it to several fire departments.</p> \n <p>When COVID-19 began spreading throughout Canada, Jia and his colleagues realized they could use some of the same technologies to combat the spread of the virus, so they began a side project. To detect a high body temperature, which is a common symptom of COVID-19, the startup used components from the visor to build Gatekeeper, a thermal-imaging system. Gatekeeper can be mounted on a wall or tripod to measure body temperature of up to five people at once.</p> \n <p>Several units have been installed in long-term-care facilities, grocery stores, and universities, Jia says.</p> \n <h3 class=\"tisubhead\">AR VISORS</h3> \n <p>Jia, a mechanical engineer, says he has been a fan of AR technology for some time. His undergraduate capstone project at <a href=\"https://www.mcmaster.ca/\">McMaster University</a>, in Hamilton, was about how AR could be used in vehicle head-up displays. Such displays, which already exist in some vehicles, can project information on the windshield, including navigation instructions, speed limit, and mileage.</p> \n <p>Jia, who earned a bachelor’s degree in automotive engineering technology, was a member of the <a href=\"https://site.ieee.org/sb-mcmaster/leadership/\">university’s IEEE student branch</a>. He later earned a master’s degree in mechanical engineering from the school.</p> \n <p>He launched Longan in 2018 with five colleagues shortly after graduating. At first, the job wasn’t full time. To get business experience under his belt as well as an understanding of how to manufacture products, he worked as a mechanical engineer for material-handling-equipment company <a href=\"https://www.skyjack.com/\">Skyjack</a> and automotive supplier <a href=\"https://www.magna.com/\">Magna</a>.</p> \n <p>Today he works full time for Longan, which has five other full-time employees and two interns.</p> \n <p>His initial idea was to develop AR glasses that integrated thermal imaging for industrial applications, such as Google Glass and Microsoft HoloLens. The company changed direction after several fires in Ontario caused major losses of life and property.</p> \n <p>“In some of these incidents, firefighters lost their lives saving people while the building was collapsing around them,” Jia says. “They needed to fight not only the fire but also [a lack of] time. Additionally, they needed to overcome obstacles, like lack of communication and terrible visibility. Their bravery inspired me.”</p> \n <p>Today’s firefighters use outdated technology, he says. He compares their equipment to cellphones of the past—which offered only basic features such as calling and texting. He wants to give first responders commercially available smart technologies to “jump-start them to the next generation of technology,” he says.</p> \n <p>“We are developing a solution,” he says, “to try and address all three of these pain points: poor visibility, communication, and data.”</p> \n <figure class=\"xlrg\" role=\"img\"> \n  <img alt=\"The Longan Vision FVS augmented-reality visor\" src=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3MzM0NQ.jpeg\"> \n   <figcaption class=\"hi-cap\">\n     Photo: Longan Vision \n   </figcaption> </img> \n </figure> \n <p>The visor [above] uses mixed-reality technology: AR and thermal imaging. A temperature sensor identifies the location of the fire. The headset allows firefighters to stream live video to a central command terminal so other responders can learn details about the structure and determine how to fight the blaze.</p> \n <p>“In a smoky building, you can’t see with the human eye,” Jia says. “Our technology can actually see through the smoke. It can give firefighters super vision, tell them where there’s a victim and where they can go past an object without bumping into it.</p> \n <p>“I believe our product with AR technology could help them prevent casualties, boost their efficiency, and send our heroes back to their families.”</p> \n <p>The images can be saved and reviewed later for training or for investigations by insurance companies. Longan Vision also plans to collect the data to create predictive models for firefighters, Jia says.</p> \n <p>The visor is compatible with many styles of firefighter helmets including models used in Asia, Europe, and the United States. Jia says there currently aren’t any other smart visors for firefighter helmets on the market.</p> \n <h3 class=\"tisubhead\">OVERCOMING OBSTACLES</h3> \n <p>As with any startup, the biggest hurdle has been a lack of funding for product research, design, development, and hiring experienced workers, Jia says.</p> \n <p>“In general,” he says, “no budget means no progress, and no progress means death to a startup.”</p> \n <p>The company began presenting its work at conferences and participating in startup competitions including those held by the IEEE Entrepreneurship initiative.</p> \n <p>Jia says those activities helped bring visibility to the company and attract investors. The founders also developed a business plan and began hiring experienced engineers “who have the passion” and are “willing to grow with the company,” he says.</p> \n <p>The first round of seed funding closed in July 2019. The company won contracts worth more than US&nbsp;$300,000, which went toward development, Jia says. One investor is <a href=\"https://www.ic.gc.ca/eic/site/101.nsf/eng/home\">Innovation Solutions Canada</a>, which helps fund the country’s startups.</p> \n <h3 class=\"tisubhead\">MULTIPERSON TEMPERATURE CHECK</h3> \n <p>When <em>The Institute</em> spoke with Jia in July to see how the company was faring during the pandemic, he told us about Gatekeeper. In addition to AR and thermal-imaging technology, it also uses a facial-recognition system.</p> \n <figure class=\"xlrg\" role=\"img\"> \n  <img alt=\"Left, photo of the Gatekeeper camera system, right, a scan showing thermal readings.\" src=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3MzM1Mg.jpeg\"> \n   <figcaption class=\"hi-cap\">\n     Photos: Longan Vision \n   </figcaption> \n   <figcaption>\n     The Gatekeeper measurement component [left] monitors the calibrated temperature of an area. The Gatekeeper camera component [right] scans individuals who cross its path and produces a visual image of each person and displays the individual’s temperature.&nbsp; \n   </figcaption> </img> \n </figure> \n <p>Jia worked with McMaster’s business incubator program, <a href=\"http://theforge.mcmaster.ca/index.php/programs/the-forge-business-incubator/\">T</a><a href=\"https://theforge.mcmaster.ca/index.php/programs/the-forge-business-incubator/\">he Forge</a>, on that detection system, which uses two thermal-imaging tools to get accurate temperature readings. The Gatekeeper measurement component monitors the calibrated temperature of an area. The Gatekeeper camera component (GCC) scans individuals who cross its path.</p> \n <p>The gathered data is sent to a computer that has a built-in image-processing program, which looks for variances between the two feeds. Information from the GCC produces a visual image of each person and displays a message on the screen identifying each individual’s temperature. Should an elevated temperature be detected, the individual can be asked to return home or seek medical attention.</p> \n <p>Jia says his system is less expensive than others on the market because Gatekeeper uses off-the-shelf components.</p> \n <h3 class=\"tisubhead\">STAR TREATMENT</h3> \n <p>Jia says he was humbled to receive the IEEE entrepreneurship recognition, which he says has opened up many doors.</p> \n <p>“It’s a big honor to be named an IEEE Entrepreneurship Star. It gives us a really big thumbs-up,” he says. “Receiving the award is also helping us on the development side. Being part of IEEE’s communities means we can actually expand our network and access potential investors and technologies that we can integrate into our [visor] system.”</p> \n <p>Another benefit that comes with the recognition is a free subscription to the <a href=\"http://xplore.ieee.org/\">IEEE Xplore Digital Library</a>—which Jia says he is thrilled about. He says much of his master’s thesis was based on research he found in the library.</p> \n <p>“We need access to papers,” he says. “That means a lot to us, to be honest. We can use the IEEE community to share our research findings on thermal imaging, AR and how it helps first responders.”</p> \n <div class=\"mobileShow\"> \n  <h3 class=\"RptHdBackBarMobile\"><span The embankment prototype in natural granular material was achieved using state of the art design and construction processes in mapping, modelling, planning and control. The entire process of building the embankment was fully autonomous. An operator was only present in the cabin for safety purposes.</em></p> \n            </blockquote> \n            <p></p> \n            <p><iframe allowfullscreen=\"\" frameborder=\"0\" height=\"349\" src=\"//www.youtube.com/embed/Wjq3Nf9rWrM\" width=\"620\"></iframe></p> \n            <p></p> \n            <p>[ <a href=\"https://rsl.ethz.ch/robots-media/heap.html\">RSL</a> ]</p> \n            <p></p> \n            <hr> \n             <p></p> \n             <blockquote> \n              <p><em>The Simulation, Systems Optimization and Robotics Group (SIM) of Technische Universität Darmstadt’s Department of Computer Science conducts research on cooperating autonomous mobile robots, biologically inspired robots and numerical optimization and control methods.</em></p> \n             </blockquote> \n             <p></p> \n             <p><iframe allowfullscreen=\"\" frameborder=\"0\" height=\"349\" src=\"//www.youtube.com/embed/gicYjz8VEEE\" width=\"620\"></iframe></p> \n             <p></p> \n             <p>[ <a href=\"https://www.sim.informatik.tu-darmstadt.de/en/index/\">SIM</a> ]</p> \n             <p></p> \n             <hr> \n              <p></p> \n              <blockquote> \n               <p><em>Starting January 1, 2021, your drone platform of choice may be severely limited by the European Union’s new drone regulations. In this short video, senseFly’s Brock Ryder explains what that means for drone programs and operators and where senseFly drones fit in the EU’s new regulatory framework. </em></p> \n              </blockquote> \n              <p></p> \n              <p><iframe allowfullscreen=\"\" frameborder=\"0\" height=\"349\" src=\"//www.youtube.com/embed/W_5mmkPlIdU\" width=\"620\"></iframe></p> \n              <p></p> \n              <p>[ <a href=\"https://www.sensefly.com/\">SenseFly</a> ]</p> \n              <p></p> \n              <hr> \n               <p></p> \n               <blockquote> \n                <p><em>Nearly every company across every industry is looking for new ways to minimize human contact, cut costs and address the labor crunch in repetitive and dangerous jobs. WSJ explores why many are looking to robots as the solution for all three.</em></p> \n               </blockquote> \n               <p></p> \n               <p><iframe allowfullscreen=\"\" frameborder=\"0\" height=\"349\" src=\"//www.youtube.com/embed/eJNSvKYKvtw\" width=\"620\"></iframe></p> \n               <p></p> \n               <p>[ <a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/channel/UCK7tptUDHh-RYDsdxO1-5QQ\">WSJ</a> ]</p> \n               <p></p> \n               <hr> \n                <p></p> \n                <p>You’ll need to prepare yourself emotionally for this video on “Examining Users’ Attitude Towards Robot Punishment.”</p> \n                <p></p> \n                <p><iframe allowfullscreen=\"\" frameborder=\"0\" height=\"349\" src=\"//www.youtube.com/embed/l2eOU5JE33I\" width=\"620\"></iframe></p> \n                <p></p> \n                <p>[ <a href=\"https://dl.acm.org/doi/abs/10.1145/3357236.3395542\">ACM</a> ]</p> \n                <p></p> \n                <hr> \n                 <p></p> \n                 <p>In this episode of the AI Podcast, Lex interviews Russ Tedrake (MIT and TRI) about biped locomotion, the DRC, home robots, and more.</p> \n                 <p></p> \n                 <p><iframe allowfullscreen=\"\" Podcast</a> ]</p> \n                 <p></p> \n                 <hr> \n                  <p></p> \n                  <img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/IeeeSpectrumFullText/~4/ZxkEXQU_Sqo\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" /> \n                 </hr> \n                </hr> \n               </hr> \n              </hr> \n             </hr> \n            </hr> \n           </hr> \n          </hr> \n         </hr> \n        </hr> \n       </hr> \n      </hr> \n     </hr> \n    </hr> \n   </hr> \n  </hr> \n </hr> \n</hr>","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Fri, 14 Aug 2020 22:30:00 +0000","feedId":1936,"bgimg":"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3NTQzMA.jpeg","linkMd5":"be06e74a825285ed6738fa2f84e8dcb9","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx12/cdn20@2020_1/2020/08/24/14-50-11-906_8ffccc83ce362e2f.webp","destWidth":1240,"destHeight":1000,"sourceBytes":333828,"destBytes":65814,"author":"Evan Ackerman","articleImgCdnMap":{"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/IeeeSpectrumFullText/~4/ZxkEXQU_Sqo":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx7/cdn53@2020_6/2020/08/24/14-50-14-937_29ab4de199d2de86.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598280594951},{"createdTime":"2020-08-24 22:49:54","updatedTime":"2020-08-24 22:49:54","title":"Social Distancing: Here’s an App for That","link":"https://spectrum.ieee.org/news-from-around-ieee/the-institute/ieee-products-services/social-distancing-heres-an-app-for-that","description":"<link href=\"/ns/interactive/0118race-to-5g/css/5g-logo-treatments.css\" rel=\"stylesheet\"> \n <style type=\"text/css\">.entry-content .tisubhead { color: #999999; font-family: verdana; font-size: 14px; font-weight: bold; letter-spacing: 1px; margin-bottom: -5px !important; text-transform: uppercase; } .tiblogopener { color: #a17e54; font-family: Theinhardt-Medium, sans-serif; letter-spacing: 1px; margin-right: 10px; font-weight: bold; text-transform: uppercase; } </style> \n <div class=\"mobileHide\"> \n  <div class=\"imgWrapper offsetLeft lt sm\"> \n   <a href=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/static/covid19-ieee-resources\"><img alt=\"IEEE COVID-19 coverage logo, link to landing page\" src=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzYxMjU3Mg.jpeg\" /></a> \n  </div> \n </div> \n <p><span class=\"tiblogopener\">THE INSTITUTE </span>One of the safety measures recommended since the beginning of the pandemic is for people to maintain a distance of at least six feet (1.5 meters) from each other. But many of us can’t eyeball the distance on our own.</p> \n <p>To help, IEEE Graduate Student Member <a href=\"https://www.linkedin.com/in/trushal-sardhara/\">Trushal Sardhara</a> and other students from various universities in India have developed the mobile app Coready! It prompts its users to maintain social distancing by alerting them when they get too close to each other.</p> \n <p>Sardhara is pursuing a master’s degree in mechatronics at the <a href=\"https://www.tuhh.de/tuhh/startseite.html\">Hamburg University of Technology</a>.</p> \n <p><a href=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/the-institute\"><em>The Institute</em></a>&nbsp;asked him about the mobile app.</p> \n <p><em>This interview has been edited and condensed for clarity.</em></p> \n <p><strong>What problem are you trying to solve? </strong></p> \n <p>Many people are finding it difficult to remember to wash their hands frequently, wear masks when stepping outside, and most importantly, maintain social distancing.</p> \n <p>We were inspired to create the mobile app—Coready!—by the <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nudge_theory\">Nudge Theory</a>, developed by behavioral economist Richard Thaler [The theory suggests that positive reinforcement&nbsp;and indirect suggestions can&nbsp;influence&nbsp;the behavior and&nbsp;decision making&nbsp;of groups or individuals.] Coready! can help protect people from contracting the virus by nudging the user when someone else [nearby] with the app is failing to maintain [a safe distance].</p> \n <p><strong>What technologies are you using? </strong></p> \n <p>Coready! uses Bluetooth Low Energy [BLE] technology to [track] if someone, who also had the app installed on their phone, is in the vicinity of the user. The mobile app continuously transmits a unique identifier using BLE technology. Other mobile devices [with the app] that come in the vicinity of this signal receive it, identify the signal, and process it in the application.</p> \n <p><strong>Explain how your project works. </strong></p> \n <p>When another person, who also has Coready! installed on his phone, is within a 1.5-meter radius of the other user, the app sends a notification to his phone as a warning. The distance between users is calculated using the strength of the normalized Bluetooth signal.</p> \n <p>The app can also determine whether the user interacts with people outside of his social bubble—who he comes into contact with the most—and violates social distancing. Coready! tracks and keeps a secure log of how many other app users he is coming into contact with and a Social Bubble Score is generated based on the information collected. The size of the user’s social bubble will be directly proportional to the score defined by the mobile app. If the user’s score is high, that means they have an increased chance of contracting the virus.</p> \n <p>Coready! also can be integrated into third-party mobile apps being used for contact tracing.</p> \n <p><strong>What challenges have you faced, and how did you overcome them? </strong></p> \n <p>The main challenge was to develop an application that can work even if it is closed or if the user’s phone is in locked mode.</p> \n <p>We encountered limitations with Android OS because it does not allow any application to run if the user closes it or locks his phone. To solve this, we used technology provided by Android OS that shows in the users’ notification bar that the application is still running.</p> \n <p><strong>What is the potential impact of the technology? </strong></p> \n <p>This application will help people maintain physical distance and limit their social bubble.</p> \n <p>It can also be used by various organizations that have a large number of employees to ensure that social distancing rules aren’t violated unknowingly in places such as corporate offices, educational institutions, and manufacturing facilities.</p> \n <p><strong>How close are you to the final product? </strong></p> \n <p>Coready! can be downloaded on Android and iOS platforms.</p> \n <p>We are continuing to update its features. We are currently working on implementing a contact tracing feature using blockchain technology to make it more robust, private, and decentralized.</p> \n <div class=\"mobileShow\"> \n  <h3 class=\"RptHdBackBarMobile\"><span class=\"BackArrowBlkBkgrd\">&lt;</span>&nbsp;<a href=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/static/covid19-ieee-resources\">Back to IEEE COVID-19 Resources</a></h3> \n </div> \n <img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/IeeeSpectrumFullText/~4/oV0OT154D8I\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" /> \n</link>","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Thu, 20 Aug 2020 18:00:00 +0000","feedId":1936,"bgimg":"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3Nzk0OQ.jpeg","linkMd5":"2823a9bc66955628e7a86527004e595b","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx29/cdn8@2020_1/2020/08/24/14-50-11-855_18dea21cc5d0bb2b.webp","destWidth":1240,"destHeight":930,"sourceBytes":50153,"destBytes":48422,"author":"The Institute’s Editorial Staff","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzYxMjU3Mg.jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx18/cdn17@2020_2/2020/08/24/14-50-14-803_b67305c597a3f2e7.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/IeeeSpectrumFullText/~4/oV0OT154D8I":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx12/cdn46@2020_1/2020/08/24/14-50-25-040_a97ceda5973aeef8.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598280594946},{"createdTime":"2020-08-24 22:49:54","updatedTime":"2020-08-24 22:49:54","title":"The AxiDraw MiniKit Is the Modern XY Plotter You Didn’t Know You Wanted ","link":"https://spectrum.ieee.org/geek-life/hands-on/the-axidraw-minikit-is-the-modern-xy-plotter-you-didnt-know-you-wanted","description":"<figure class=\"xlrg\" role=\"img\"> \n <img alt=\"Illustration of the AxiDraw MiniKit\" src=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3NzUyMg.jpeg\"> \n  <div class=\"ai\"> \n   <figcaption class=\"hi-cap\">\n     Illustration: James Provost \n   </figcaption> \n  </div> </img> \n</figure> \n<p><strong>I’ll be honest: when the original</strong> <a href=\"https://axidraw.com/\">AxiDraw</a> came out from <a href=\"https://www.evilmadscientist.com/\">Evil Mad Scientist Laboratories</a> in 2016, I thought it was cute but a curiosity. My thought was that if you were going to play around with reintroducing the venerable technology of the pen-and-paper X-Y plotter, why not do something really different, like the <a href=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/geek-life/hands-on/the-iboardbot-brings-xy-plotters-into-the-cloud-age\">iBoardBot remote-controlled erasable miniature whiteboard</a>? And the AxiDraw’s US $475 price tag felt steep for a spindly-looking contraption that required the user to manually position each sheet of paper, one at a time.</p> \n<p>Boy, was I wrong. The AxiDraw took off—folks have embraced it for creating <a href=\"https://mattdesl.svbtle.com/pen-plotter-1\">generative art</a>, <a href=\"https://ubir.buffalo.edu/xmlui/bitstream/handle/10477/78136/Eadie_buffalo_0656M_15885.pdf?sequence=3\">making microfluidic systems with wax</a>, <a href=\"https://blog.adafruit.com/2016/11/16/axidraw-modified-to-laser-cut-and-uv-light-paint/\">laser cutting,</a> <a href=\"https://www.researchgate.net/figure/a-Photograph-of-a-computer-numerical-control-CNC-drawing-machine-AxiDraw-V3_fig4_340864484\">engraving of all types</a>, <a href=\"https://youtu.be/oPfZdZaTGG4\">testing computer mice</a>, and much more. In short, it became a general-purpose platform for all sorts of experiments, and the reason was also the reason for that high price tag: the AxiDraw’s consistent precision. But there’s a way to get that precision and start experimenting for less money, albeit with a couple of trade-offs, with the $325 <a href=\"https://shop.evilmadscientist.com/productsmenu/924\">AxiDraw MiniKit</a>, which came out late last year.</p> \n<p>The first trade-off is the size: the MiniKit’s drawing area is 15 by 10 centimeters, while the standard AxiDraw can handle up to 30 by 21.8 cm. But the MiniKit’s smaller size may be a boon to some, fitting better on a desk or workbench, and easier to transport. The second trade-off is time, specifically yours, because the MiniKit is the first AxiDraw to come in build-it-yourself kit form.</p> \n<figure class=\"xlrg\" role=\"img\"> \n <img alt=\"Illustration of the parts of the AxiDraw MiniKit.\" src=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3NzYyNA.jpeg\"> \n  <div class=\"ai\"> \n   <figcaption class=\"hi-cap\">\n     Illustration: James Provost \n   </figcaption> \n   <figcaption> \n    <strong>Electromechanical Medley:</strong> The AxiDraw MiniKit takes a few hours to assemble and adjust. Specialized tools, such as a Torx L-wrench, are included, but you must supply some conventional tools, such as a screwdriver. The aluminum rails have sharp edges that require care during assembly, but end caps are included to protect users. Drawing implements can be held either vertically, or at a 45-degree angle, which is more suitable for things like fountain pens. The MiniKit is controlled via free software that can plot vector files, including stipple portraits or handwriting fonts. \n   </figcaption> \n  </div> </img> \n</figure> \n<p>As someone who is a lot more confident with circuits than cogs, assembling the MiniKit looked a little daunting at first. All kinds of wheels, minutely varied screws and washers, and various mystery components were crammed into the box. Fortunately, the instructions are some of the most thoughtfully detailed that I’ve ever seen, with many pictures and tips on identifying the M3x8 button-head screw versus the M4x8 torx tapping screw.</p> \n<p>The MiniKit’s x-axis is formed by one long fixed rail. A movable chassis runs along this rail, and it supports the y-axis rail in turn, to which the penholder is affixed. Construction took a few hours: The trickiest parts were getting the toothed drive belt in position with adequate tension and adjusting the wheels that grip the MiniKit’s rails so that things can move freely but without any looseness.</p> \n<p>The MiniKit uses two stepper motors to position the penholder, but rather than having the motors drive motion along the x and y axes directly, both motors are placed on opposite ends of the x-axis rail. The drive belt is looped through the movable chassis and along the y-axis in such a way that turning one motor while holding the other in place causes the penholder to move at a 45 degree angle to the rails—if the MiniKit were a compass, this would be along the NE to SW line. Turning the other motor, while holding the first one still, moves the penholder along the NW to SE line. This means the axis controlled by each motor is effectively rotated through 45 degrees, so the MiniKit’s microcontroller takes care of mapping incoming x-y coordinates to the appropriate motor signals.</p> \n<p>The MiniKit is primarily controlled from a desktop or laptop using an extension to <a href=\"https://inkscape.org/\">Inkscape</a>, an open source vector-drawing tool. The software is also required for the final assembly step: calibrating the servo that raises and lowers the penholder. However, many other software options for controlling AxiDraw plotters exist, and it’s <a href=\"https://github.com/evil-mad/AxiDraw-Processing\">pretty straightforward to write your own code</a> using the <a href=\"https://processing.org/\">Processing language</a>, which is designed for creating ­computer-assisted and computer-generated art, or write a Python script.</p> \n<p><iframe allowfullscreen=\"\" frameborder=\"0\" height=\"349\" src=\"//www.youtube.com/embed/U2i1SpaxfKw\" width=\"620\"></iframe></p> \n<p>Once I had the servo calibrated and pushed the penholder by hand to its home position in the upper left corner of a page, I fitted a roller-ball pen into the MiniKit—you have a choice of holding pens in a vertical or slanted position, the slant being ­especially suitable if you want to try, say, a fountain pen. The test graphic rendered perfectly, first time, scratching out a welcome message in a font that’s a believable imitation of cursive handwriting (or at least how we used to write cursive, before keyboards caused our penmanship to atrophy).</p> \n<p>Evil Mad Scientist Laboratories also offers a handy free stand-alone utility program called StippleGen. You can feed an image into the utility, and it creates a version made up of monochromatic stippled dots of various sizes. The number of dots and their size range is controllable, and the program also tries to determine the best, shortest, path between the dots, so as to minimize the amount of time that the AxiDraw spends moving around.</p> \n<figure class=\"xlrg\" role=\"img\"> \n <img alt=\"Illustration of the belt motors of the MiniKit.  \" src=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3NzY0NA.jpeg\"> \n  <div class=\"ai\"> \n   <figcaption class=\"hi-cap\">\n     Illustration: James Provost \n   </figcaption> \n   <figcaption> \n    <strong>Axial tilt:</strong> The rotations of the MiniKit stepper motors do not directly correspond to the x and y axes. Instead, its belt arrangement means that holding one motor still and turning the other moves the drawing head along either a or b axes, as shown. The onboard microcontroller takes care of the math required to transform x and y coordinates to a and b coordinates. \n   </figcaption> \n  </div> </img> \n</figure> \n<p>The resulting file can then be imported into Inkscape and plotted. Warning though: This can take quite a while. For my first test I used a photo of some Irish passage graves, which needed about 5,000 dots to be recognizable: It took about 2 hours to complete drawing the image, taking over a second to draw each dot and move to the next. (I was able to shave quite a bit off that time by ­reducing how high the MiniKit lifted the pen between dots in later tests.) The stipple plots work best with faces, which tend to be discernible at lower dot counts than other photographic subjects, no doubt due to the specialized facial-recognition neural hardware in our brains.</p> \n<p>In short, the AxiDraw MiniKit rewards experimentation—there are many things to try, but you don’t have to master them all before you get rewarding results. And if nothing else, it should make addressing envelopes easier!</p> \n<img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/IeeeSpectrumFullText/~4/8VoMOTE752Y\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" />","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Fri, 21 Aug 2020 19:00:00 +0000","feedId":1936,"bgimg":"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3NzUzOA.jpeg","linkMd5":"f0cc1b320090c9b9deb94c7081fb252d","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx6/cdn72@2020_4/2020/08/24/14-50-11-884_1cf5af38fb774cbd.webp","destWidth":1240,"destHeight":990,"sourceBytes":195168,"destBytes":132632,"author":"Stephen Cass","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3NzUyMg.jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx33/cdn1@2020_2/2020/08/24/14-50-12-979_ced632f6cc29bddf.webp","https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3NzYyNA.jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx5oss/cdn68@2020_2/2020/08/24/14-50-11-676_406986445fa6c7ef.webp","https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3NzY0NA.jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx25/cdn69@2020_3/2020/08/24/14-50-14-922_353c5f537b1ada6c.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/IeeeSpectrumFullText/~4/8VoMOTE752Y":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx32/cdn78@2020_3/2020/08/24/14-50-13-085_2061de32556185f7.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598280594951},{"createdTime":"2020-08-24 22:49:54","updatedTime":"2020-08-24 22:49:54","title":"Minuscule RoBeetle Turns Liquid Methanol Into Muscle Power","link":"https://spectrum.ieee.org/automaton/robotics/robotics-hardware/robeetle-liquid-methanol","description":"<p>It’s no secret that one of the most significant constraints on robots is power. Most robots need lots of it, and it has to come from somewhere, with that somewhere usually being a battery because there simply aren’t many other good options. Batteries, however, are famous for having poor energy density, and the smaller your robot is, the more of a problem this becomes. And the issue with batteries goes beyond the battery itself, but also carries over into all the other components&nbsp;that it takes to turn the stored energy into useful work, which again is a particular problem for small-scale robots.</p> \n<p>In a paper published this week in <em>Science Robotics</em>, researchers from the&nbsp;University of Southern California, in Los Angeles, demonstrate RoBeetle, an 88-milligram four legged robot that runs entirely on methanol, a power-dense liquid fuel. Without any electronics at all, it uses an exceptionally clever bit of mechanical autonomy to convert methanol vapor directly into forward motion, one millimeter-long step at a time.</p> \n<!--nextpage--> \n<p><iframe allowfullscreen=\"\" frameborder=\"0\" height=\"349\" src=\"//www.youtube.com/embed/vd6tN19wilQ\" width=\"620\"></iframe></p> \n<p>It’s not entirely clear from the video how the robot actually works, so let’s go through how it’s put together, and then look at the actuation cycle.</p> \n<figure class=\"xlrg\" role=\"img\"> \n <img alt=\"Robot beetle powered by methanol\" src=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3ODMyOQ.jpeg\"> \n  <figcaption class=\"hi-cap\">\n    Image: Science Robotics \n  </figcaption> \n  <figcaption>\n    RoBeetle (A) uses a methanol-based actuation mechanism (B).&nbsp;The robot’s body&nbsp;(C)&nbsp;includes the fuel tank subassembly (D), a&nbsp;tank lid, transmission, and sliding shutter (E), bottom side of the sliding shutter (F), nickel-titanium-platinum composite wire and leaf spring (G), and&nbsp;front legs and hind legs with bioinspired backward-oriented claws (H). \n  </figcaption> </img> \n</figure> \n<p>The body of RoBeetle is a boxy fuel tank that you can fill with methanol by poking a syringe through a fuel inlet hole. It’s a quadruped, more or less, with fixed hind legs and two front legs attached to a single transmission that moves them both at once in a sort of rocking forward and up followed by backward and down motion. The transmission is hooked up to a leaf spring that’s tensioned to always pull the legs backward, such that when the robot isn’t being actuated, the spring and transmission keep its front legs more or less vertical and allow the robot to stand. Those horns are primarily there to hold the leaf spring in place, but they’ve got little hooks that can carry stuff, too.</p> \n<p>The actuator itself is a nickel-titanium (NiTi) shape-memory alloy (SMA), which is just a wire that gets longer when it heats up and then shrinks back down when it cools. SMAs are fairly common and used for all kinds of things, but what makes this particular SMA a little different is that it’s been messily coated with platinum. The “messily” part is important for a reason that we’ll get to in just a second.</p> \n<aside class=\"inlay pullquote xlrg\">\n  The way that the sliding vent is attached to the transmission is the really clever bit about this robot, because it means that the motion of the wire itself is used to modulate the flow of fuel through a purely mechanical system. Essentially, it’s an actuator and a sensor at the same time. \n</aside> \n<p>One end of the SMA wire is attached to the middle of the leaf spring, while the other end runs above the back of the robot where it’s stapled to an anchor block on the robot’s rear end. With the SMA wire hooked up but not actuated (i.e., cold rather than warm), it’s short enough that the leaf spring gets pulled back, rocking the legs forward and up. The last component is embedded in the robot’s back, right along the spine and directly underneath the SMA actuator. It’s a sliding vent attached to the transmission, so that the vent is open when the SMA wire is cold and the leaf spring is pulled back, and closed when the SMA wire is warm and the leaf spring is relaxed. The way that the sliding vent is attached to the transmission is the really clever bit about this robot, because it means that the motion of the wire itself is used to modulate the flow of fuel through a purely mechanical system. Essentially, it’s an actuator and a sensor at the same time.</p> \n<p>The actuation cycle that causes the robot to walk begins with a full fuel tank and a cold SMA wire. There’s tension on the leaf spring, pulling the transmission back and rocking the legs forward and upward. The transmission also pulls the sliding vent into the open position, allowing methanol vapor to escape up out of the fuel tank and into the air, where it wafts past the SMA wire that runs directly above the vent.&nbsp;</p> \n<p>The platinum facilitates a reaction of the methanol (CH<sub>3</sub>OH) with oxygen in the air (combustion, although not the dramatic flaming and explosive kind) to generate a couple of water molecules and some carbon dioxide plus a bunch of heat, and this is where the messy platinum coating is important, because messy means lots of surface area for the platinum to interact with as much methanol as possible. In just a second or two the temperature of the SMA wire skyrockets from 50 to 100 <span>º</span>C and it expands, allowing the leaf spring about 0.1 mm of slack. As the leaf spring relaxes, the transmission moves the legs backwards and downwards, and the robot pulls itself forward about 1.2 mm. At the same time, the transmission is closing off the sliding vent, cutting off the supply of methanol vapor. Without the vapor reacting with the platinum and generating heat, in about a second and a half, the SMA wire cools down. As it does, it shrinks, pulling on the leaf spring and starting the cycle over again. Top speed is 0.76 mm/s (0.05 body-lengths per second).</p> \n<p>An interesting environmental effect is that the speed of the robot can be enhanced by a gentle breeze. This is because air moving over the SMA wire cools it down a bit faster while also blowing away any residual methanol from around the vents, shutting down the reaction more completely. RoBeetle can carry more than its own body weight in fuel, and it takes approximately 155 minutes for a full tank of methanol to completely evaporate. It’s worth noting that despite the very high energy density of methanol, this is actually a stupendously inefficient way of powering a robot, with an estimated end-to-end efficiency of just 0.48 percent. Not 48 percent, mind you, but 0.48 percent, while in general, powering SMAs with electricity is much more efficient.</p> \n<p>However, you have to look at the entire system that would be necessary to deliver that electricity, and for a robot as small as RoBeetle, the researchers say that it’s basically impossible. The lightest commercially available battery and power supply that would deliver enough juice to heat up an SMA actuator weighs about 800 mg, nearly 10 times the total weight of RoBeetle itself. From that perspective, RoBeetle’s efficiency is actually pretty good.&nbsp;</p> \n<figure class=\"xlrg\" role=\"img\"> \n <img alt=\"RoBeetle\" src=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3ODM0OQ.jpeg\"> \n  <figcaption class=\"hi-cap\">\n    Image:&nbsp;A. Kitterman/Science Robotics; adapted from R.L.T./MIT \n  </figcaption> \n  <figcaption>\n    Comparison of various untethered microrobots and bioinspired soft robots that use different power and actuation strategies. \n  </figcaption> </img> \n</figure> \n<p>There are some other downsides to RoBeetle we should mention—it can only move forwards, not backwards, and it can’t steer. Its speed isn’t adjustable, and once it starts walking, it’ll walk until it either breaks or runs out of fuel. The researchers have some ideas about the speed, at least, pointing out that increasing the speed of fuel delivery by using pressurized liquid fuels like butane or propane would increase the actuator output frequency. And the frequency, amplitude, and efficiency of the SMAs themselves can be massively increased “by arranging multiple fiber-like thin artificial muscles in hierarchical configurations similar to those observed in sarcomere-based animal muscle,” making RoBeetle even more beetle-like.</p> \n<p>As for sensing, RoBeetle’s 230-mg payload is enough to carry passive sensors, but getting those sensors to usefully interact with the robot itself to enable any kind of autonomy remains a challenge. Mechanically intelligence is certainly possible, though, and we can imagine RoBeetle adopting some of the same sorts of systems that have been proposed for <a href=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/automaton/robotics/space-robots/jpl-design-for-a-clockwork-rover-to-explore-venus\">the clockwork rover that JPL wants to use for Venus exploration</a>. The researchers also mention how RoBeetle could potentially serve as a model for microbots capable of aerial locomotion, which is something we’d very much like to see.</p> \n<p>“<a href=\"https://robotics.sciencemag.org/content/5/45/eaba0015\">An 88-milligram insect-scale autonomous crawling robot driven by a catalytic artificial muscle</a>,” by Xiufeng Yang, Longlong Chang, and Néstor O. Pérez-Arancibia from University of Southern California, in Los Angeles, was published in <em>Science Robotics</em>.</p> \n<img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/IeeeSpectrumFullText/~4/vJpIEcsOJKU\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" />","descriptionType":"html","publishedDate":"Thu, 20 Aug 2020 18:26:00 +0000","feedId":1936,"bgimg":"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3ODM1MQ.jpeg","linkMd5":"c4fd9790c2d4f6a48ab9ae62b562ad77","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx24/cdn88@2020_1/2020/08/24/14-50-13-830_2fc8d199f737bd39.webp","destWidth":1240,"destHeight":823,"sourceBytes":196067,"destBytes":49770,"author":"Evan Ackerman","articleImgCdnMap":{"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3ODMyOQ.jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx2/cdn50@2020_2/2020/08/24/14-50-13-176_3ea4ebd261bb4fa6.webp","https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3ODM0OQ.jpeg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx16/cdn5@2020_5/2020/08/24/14-50-13-081_2e9962a62332841f.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/IeeeSpectrumFullText/~4/vJpIEcsOJKU":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx1/cdn73@2020_6/2020/08/24/14-50-14-196_54fb2397e6acc596.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598280594951},{"createdTime":"2020-08-24 22:49:54","updatedTime":"2020-08-24 22:49:54","title":"Turning Bricks Into Supercapacitors","link":"https://spectrum.ieee.org/energywise/energy/batteries-storage/turning-red-bricks-into-energy-storage-devices","description":"<p><span id=\"docs-internal-guid-32bb6e05-7fff-53e5-2e80-ea3460b6a0d3\"><span>As solar panels and wind turbines multiply, the big problem&nbsp;is how to store all the&nbsp;excess electricity produced when the sun is up or the wind blowing&nbsp;so that it can be used at other times. Potential solutions have been suggested&nbsp;in many forms, including massive battery banks, fast-spinning flywheels, and underground vaults of air. Now a team of researchers say a classic construction material—the red fired brick—could be a contender in the quest for energy storage.</span></span></p> \n<p><span id=\"docs-internal-guid-32bb6e05-7fff-53e5-2e80-ea3460b6a0d3\"><span>The common brick is porous like a sponge, and it’s red color comes from pigmentation that is</span></span><span><span>&nbsp;rich in iron oxide. Both features provide ideal conditions for growing and hosting conductive polymers, <a href=\"http://artsci.wustl.edu/faculty-staff/julio-m-darcy\">Julio D’Arcy</a> and colleagues have found. The team at Washington University in St. Louis transformed basic blocks into supercapacitors that can illuminate a light-emitting diode.&nbsp;</span></span></p> \n<p>Supercapacitors are of&nbsp;<span>interest because, unlike batteries, they can deliver blindingly fast bursts of power&nbsp;and they recharge quickly. The downside is that, kilogram for kilogram,&nbsp;they store relatively little energy compared to batteries. In an electric vehicle, a supercapacitor supports acceleration, but the lithium-ion module is what provides power for hundreds of miles. Yet many scientists and technology developers are hoping supercapacitors can replace conventional batteries in many applications, owing to the steep environmental toll of mining and disposing of metals.&nbsp;</span></p> \n<p><span>The building brick<span>&nbsp;<a href=\"http://www.nature.com/articles/s41467-020-17708-1\">proof-of-concept project</a> presents new possibilities for the world’s many brick walls and structures, said D’Arcy, an assistant professor of chemistry at Washington University. Rooftop solar panels connected by wires could charge the bricks, which in turn could provide in-house backup power for emergency lighting or other applications.</span></span></p> \n<figure class=\"xlrg\" role=\"img\"> \n <img alt=\"A brick supercapacitor coated with a simple five-minute epoxy is immersed in water, demonstrating the device's impermeability.\" src=\"https://spectrum.ieee.org/image/MzY3NDYwOQ.jpeg\"> \n  <figcaption class=\"hi-cap\">\n    Image:&nbsp;D'Arcy Laboratory/Washington University in St. Louis \n  </figcaption> \n  <figcaption>\n    A brick supercapacitor coated with a simple five-minute epoxy is immersed in water, demonstrating the device's impermeability. \n  </figcaption> </img>\n</figure> \n<p><span><span>“If we’re successful [in scaling up], you’d no longer need batteries in your house,” he said by phone. “The brick itself would be the battery.”</span></span></p> \n<p><span id=\"docs-internal-guid-32bb6e05-7fff-53e5-2e80-ea3460b6a0d3\"><span>The novel device, described in </span><em>Nature Communications </em><span>on Tuesday, is a far cry from the megawatt-scale storage projects underway in places like California’s desert and China’s countryside. But D’Arcy said the paper shows, for the first time, that bricks can store electrical energy. It offers “food for thought” in a sector that’s searching for ideas, he noted.&nbsp;</span></span></p> \n<p><span id=\"docs-internal-guid-32bb6e05-7fff-53e5-2e80-ea3460b6a0d3\"><span>Researchers began by buying armfuls of 65-cent red bricks at a big-box hardware store. At the lab, they studied the material’s microstructure and filled the bricks’ many pores with vapors. Next, bricks went into an oven heated to 160° Celsius. The iron oxide&nbsp;triggered a chemical reaction, coating the bricks’ cavities with thin layers of PEDOT, the polymer known as poly(3,4- ethylenedioxythiophene).&nbsp;</span></span></p> \n<p><span id=\"docs-internal-guid-32bb6e05-7fff-53e5-2e80-ea3460b6a0d3\"><span>Bricks emerged from the oven with a blackish-blue hue—and the ability to conduct electricity.</span></span></p> \n<p><span id=\"docs-internal-guid-32bb6e05-7fff-53e5-2e80-ea3460b6a0d3\"><span>D’Arcy’s team then attached copper leads to two coated bricks. To stop the blocks from shorting out while stacked together, the</span></span><span><span>&nbsp;researchers separated&nbsp;the blocks with a thin plastic sheet of polypropylene. A sulfuric-acid based solution was used as a liquid electrolyte, and the bricks were connected via&nbsp;the copper leads to a AAA battery for about one minute. Once charged, the bricks could power a white LED for 11 minutes.&nbsp;</span></span><span>&nbsp;</span></p> \n<p><span id=\"docs-internal-guid-32bb6e05-7fff-53e5-2e80-ea3460b6a0d3\"><span>If applied to 50 bricks, the supercapacitor could power 3 watts’ worth of lights for about 50 minutes, D’Arcy said. The current set-up can be recharged 10,000 times and still retain about 90 percent of its original capacitance. Researchers are&nbsp;developing the polymer’s chemistry further in an effort to reach 100,000 recharges.&nbsp;</span></span></p> \n<p><span id=\"docs-internal-guid-32bb6e05-7fff-53e5-2e80-ea3460b6a0d3\"><span>However, the&nbsp;St. Louis researchers are not alone in the quest to use&nbsp;everyday (if unusual) materials to make supercapacitors.</span></span></p> \n<p><span id=\"docs-internal-guid-32bb6e05-7fff-53e5-2e80-ea3460b6a0d3\"><span>In Scotland, a team at the University of Glasgow <a href=\"http://www.gla.ac.uk/news/headline_722512_en.html\">has developed</a> a flexible device that can be fully charged with human sweat. Researchers applied a thin layer of PEDOT to a piece of polyester cellulose cloth that absorbs the wearer’s perspiration, creating an electrochemical reaction and generating electricity. The idea is that these coated cloths could power wearable electronics, using a tiny amount of sweat to keep running.</span></span></p> \n<p><span id=\"docs-internal-guid-32bb6e05-7fff-53e5-2e80-ea3460b6a0d3\"><span>The Indian Institute of Technology-Hyderabad is exploring the use of corn husks in high-voltage supercapacitors. India’s corn producing states generate substantial amounts of husk waste, which researchers say can be converted into activated carbon electrodes. The biomass offers a potentially cheaper and simpler alternative to electrodes derived from polymers and similar materials, according to a <a href=\"http://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/abs/pii/S0378775320306911\">recent study</a> in </span><em>Journal of Power Sources.</em></span></p> \n<p><span id=\"docs-internal-guid-32bb6e05-7fff-53e5-2e80-ea3460b6a0d3\"><span>However, to really make inroads into the dominance of batteries, where a chemical reaction drives creation of a voltage,</span></span><span id=\"docs-internal-guid-32bb6e05-7fff-53e5-2e80-ea3460b6a0d3\"><span>&nbsp;supercapacitors will need to significantly increase their energy density. D’Arcy said his electrically charged bricks are “two orders of magnitude away” from lithium-ion batteries, in terms of the amount of energy they can store.&nbsp;</span></span></p> \n<p><span id=\"docs-internal-guid-32bb6e05-7fff-53e5-2e80-ea3460b6a0d3\"><span>“That’s another thing we’re trying to do—make our polymer store more energy,” he said. “A lot of groups are trying to do this,” he added, “but they didn’t do it in bricks.”</span></span></p>\n<img src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/IeeeSpectrumFullText/~4/YHXcNziIQOY\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\"
